# 3270 emulator?

_177 messages · 14 participants · 2011-06_

---

### 3270 emulator?

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2011-06-03T10:31:38+12:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<94qhabFoamU1@mid.individual.net>`

```
COBOLlers concerned with improving the user experience (if there are any) 
might be interested in this...

http://www.youtube.com/watch?v=p92QfWOw88I

As someone who has a toe in this water  (through tiled app development for 
phones) I can report it is great fun to program.

(But you DO need to understand OOP to do it...)

Of course, it is much easier to dismiss it as "unnecessary fluff" when 
everybody knows that green text on a black screen is all anybody REALLY 
needs.

Pete.
```

```

"Alistair Maclean" <alistair.j.l.maclean@gmail.com> wrote in message 
news:cf1f4589-2841-488f-a126-42f132bf9f18@gh5g2000vbb.googlegroups.com...
On Jun 3, 5:36 pm, SkippyPB <swieg...@Nospam.neo.rr.com> wrote:
> On Fri, 3 Jun 2011 04:51:12 -0700 (PDT), Alistair Maclean
>
…[43 more quoted lines elided]…
> Steve

>Two-thumb typing? Sooooo....klutzy. I've just mastered two fingers (on
>seperate hands).

Yeah... me too :-) I'm quite proud that I can reach 40 WPM (after only using 
keyboards for 40 odd years). This is about HALF the speed of a competent 
typist. Every time I place "learn to type properly" on my TODO list, it gets 
pushed off by other stuff...

I like touch surfaces and I think (hope...) voice input will be the go 
instead of typing. (Ideal for old farts like me). Of course, the rising 
generation who this technology is aimed at, already EXPECT it from using 
their phones and if you ever watch a teenager texting with their thumbs you 
have to admire the skill. I saw a boy in a cafe the other day texting while 
talking to his friends. Never looked at the screen once...

The point is that a new generation of users are going to demand and expect 
this kind of interface. A new breed of programmers will deliver it. (And 
occasionally, a few of the "old breed" will make the jump and get into it 
too. It really is great fun to work with and it is less complex than you 
might expect. The toolsets are superb and things can be built quite 
rapidly.)

Pete.
```

```
On Jun 4, 12:53 pm, Howard Brazee <how...@brazee.net> wrote:
> On Sat, 4 Jun 2011 09:49:49 +1200, "Pete Dashwood"
>
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
> have to disagree with you, old dogs and new tricks you know.

I had an OS/2 system with voice input nearly a quarter of a century
ago. It recognised most stuff but was not a lot of use. It was
especially useless for programming and for data input. It was marginal
for text input and was more trouble than it was worth.

Dragon, IBM, MS and others had voice commands many years ago, they
were not used.

Voice input in an office environment or on the street is confused by
noise.

There are places where it is useful. 30 years ago some meat works had
it installed for grading work because the workers could not use their
hands for inputting. Apart from using the hands for other purposes
there was a risk of contamination.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: 3270 emulator?

_(reply depth: 7)_

- **From:** Michael Wojcik <mwojcik@newsguy.com>
- **Date:** 2011-06-06T11:00:41-04:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<isit11217mr@news7.newsguy.com>`
- **In-Reply-To:** `<fa131a0c-acad-4b51-9a01-44539b18adc8@p9g2000prh.googlegroups.com>`
- **References:** `<94qhabFoamU1@mid.individual.net> <85373ac0-92bc-4e7c-b3ad-6d0595e21d22@s9g2000yqm.googlegroups.com> <ka3iu6516gnkgoo95itjnbmvvv51vdokt7@4ax.com> <cf1f4589-2841-488f-a126-42f132bf9f18@gh5g2000vbb.googlegroups.com> <94t37uF98iU1@mid.individual.net> <1g0ju6l4gimc5uu9n5l6igu4nuublehncn@4ax.com> <fa131a0c-acad-4b51-9a01-44539b18adc8@p9g2000prh.googlegroups.com>`

```
Richard wrote:
> 
> I had an OS/2 system with voice input nearly a quarter of a century
> ago. It recognised most stuff but was not a lot of use. It was
> especially useless for programming and for data input. It was marginal
> for text input and was more trouble than it was worth.

Agreed. I had OS/2 Warp with voice recognition as well, along with
everyone else in the office where I was working when it came out. I
don't recall anyone using the VR feature for more than a day or so.

> There are places where it is useful. 30 years ago some meat works had
> it installed for grading work because the workers could not use their
> hands for inputting. Apart from using the hands for other purposes
> there was a risk of contamination.

There are some language-learning software applications that use VR to
test and drill oral knowledge of the language. I have one for
Japanese. It's a decent way to practice pronunciation. Again, though,
it's a specialized application.

Of course, anyone who really cares one way or the other about
alternative input methods can look at the tons of UI/UX/HCI/HF
research on the subject, going back at least to the 1970s. Voice
recognition is pretty tame compared to some of the things people have
done, for example with eye-tracking UIs.
```

Leaving aside the Godwinising of the thread for a mere momement I am 
reminded of a classic example of joke-as-stress-reversor:

In Berlin, in 1938, old Mr (insert recogniseably Hebraic name here) saw a 
storm-trooper intimidating the passers-by with a large, violent, 
barely-contained canine.  Hoping to calm things the elderly Son of David 
approached and, in kindly tones, 'made nice' by saying 'What a lovely dog 
that is? Such fine legs, such a keen eye... tell me, what kind of breed is 
he?'

The SS-man snarled back 'Isn't it obvious?  He is a cross between a 
mongrel, unsuitable for living among humans, and a Jew!.'

'How very nice!', responded the geriatric, '... so what you're telling me 
is that he's related to both of us!'.

DD

```


"Howard Brazee" <howard@brazee.net> wrote in message 
news:1g0ju6l4gimc5uu9n5l6igu4nuublehncn@4ax.com...
> On Sat, 4 Jun 2011 09:49:49 +1200, "Pete Dashwood"
> <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> Are you saying that voice input is ideal for old farts?

It is pretty good for THIS old fart.

Quite right, Howard. I was extending my personal experience as an old fart 
to ALL old farts. (It's been a while since I posted here; I forgot the 
pedantry of this forum, sorry.)

> If so, I
> have to disagree with you, old dogs and new tricks you know.

My eyesight is deteriorating  more rapidly and I find it increasingly 
difficult to type. (And, as noted elsewhere, I never was much good at it...)

I played with a Dragon system for a while and found it took a couple of days 
to get used to, but after that it was very useful, for both reading and 
writing. MS Word has had text-to-speech for many years but few people use 
it. Dragon has both and it is pretty good.

I could see a GUI panel with a "voice input" tile on it, that might save 
considerable stress on the thumbs. :-)

The first time I ever saw voice recognition was on a CDC Cyber mainframe in 
1972. It took the entire computing capacity of what was then one of the most 
powerfulcomputers in the world, to run voice recognition. And it was around 
85% accurate. The Dragon system runs on a PC and is around 92% accurate.

It seems reasonable to conclude that this will improve and we should 
eventually get 100%, or so close as doesn't matter.

The main drawback with such systems is that they have had to be "trained" 
for the nuances and accent of a specific speaker, although I understand 
there are some new systems now which claim this is no longer necessary. 
Perhaps it will be like handwriting recognition where a dedicated team of 
Russian mathematicians formulated algorithms that brought it to pocket 
computers a few years back. I don't know. But I do know I'll be in line for 
it when it hits 100%... :-)

As for teaching dogs (old or new) new tricks... the meatiness of the reward 
has much to do with it. :-)

(Am I the only old fart in the World who is an "early adopter"...?)

Pete.
```

- **Message-ID:** `<isit12417mr@news7.newsguy.com>`

- **References:** `<94qhabFoamU1@mid.individual.net> <85373ac0-92bc-4e7c-b3ad-6d0595e21d22@s9g2000yqm.googlegroups.com> <ka3iu6516gnkgoo95itjnbmvvv51vdokt7@4ax.com> <cf1f4589-2841-488f-a126-42f132bf9f18@gh5g2000vbb.googlegroups.com> <94t37uF98iU1@mid.individual.net> <1g0ju6l4gimc5uu9n5l6igu4nuublehncn@4ax.com> <94thd7F8k9U1@mid.individual.net> <72599b39-ea2f-4e67-bb24-7142c060dcc3@j25g2000vbr.googlegroups.com>`

```
Alistair Maclean wrote:
> 
>> The main drawback with such systems is that they have had to be "trained"
>> for the nuances and accent of a specific speaker, although I understand
>> there are some new systems now which claim this is no longer necessary.

Those would be systems with unsupervised or minimally-supervised
learning algorithms. There's been a lot of progress in that area in
the last decade or so, often by using the huge data corpora available
these days to match fragments of the user's initial input and predict
where it's going, until the system can build a user-specific model.

>> Perhaps it will be like handwriting recognition where a dedicated team of
>> Russian mathematicians formulated algorithms that brought it to pocket
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> Was that using fuzzy logic (he enquired innocently)?

>From my reading, fuzzy logic is not currently in vogue for most
natural language processing (NLP) tasks. Fuzzy logic is really just
another alternative to various other mathematical systems, such as
dynamic programming, and in this problem domain fuzzy logic may not be
particularly amenable to analysis. (It's often more useful when it's
easier to constrain input and outputs, eg in transportation routing.)

Most of the work I've read recently uses Maximum-Entropy Markov Models
with heterogeneous features and some form of optimization, such as
Expectation Maximization.
```

Alistair Maclean wrote:
> On Jun 6, 4:32 pm, Michael Wojcik <mwoj...@newsguy.com> wrote:
> 
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> Thanks for putting me straight on the use of Markov models.

Just remember that's based on the NLP research I've read recently.
That's maybe a dozen articles, a couple dozen more abstracts, and a
recent textbook. So take it with a healthy dose of salt.

> I asked
> the question with a small degree of foreknowledge having read a book
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> detail how fuzzy logic had been used to improve voice recognition.

Voice recognition, or speech recognition? The former is typically a
biometric task - decide if this voice belongs to a known person. The
latter is trying to make sense of spoken language.

It's quite possible that fuzzy logic was the tool de jour for speech
recognition a while back. I just haven't run into any mention of it
recently in that context.
```

```
Alistair Maclean wrote:
> On Jun 9, 11:11 pm, "Pete Dashwood"
> <dashw...@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:
…[30 more quoted lines elided]…
> positive personality.

 Sometimes, a problem or disappointment actually CAN be an opportunity. 
Often the way we perceive things has a huge impact on the way we deal with 
them.

When you are feeling down it is easy to see every setback as major (whether 
it is or not) and energy seems to dissipate and you just feel tired... 
Change your mind about the situation, see how it can be turned to advantage 
(there are very few situations which have absolutely NO positive outcomes 
for SOMEBODY :-)), and suddenly you feel revitalised and ready to try the 
new approach.

I'm tired and down at the moment but I am experienced enough to recognise it 
and I know I need to do something about it. I have myself on notice and have 
a planned schedule that will bring me out of it and restore my normal 
optimism and energy :-). (Meantime, I'm actually enjoying having time out 
from work and refurbishing the kitchen :-)) I probably need a holiday but 
can't afford one at the moment. (besides I spent what "disposable income" I 
had on the kitchen... :-)) My point is that all of us have ups and downs. 
Recognising them, and doing something about them, rather than just letting a 
dwindling downward spiral take over, is a skill that is useful to develop.

Sometimes, you cannot change a condition, but you can change your mind about 
the condition.

Pete.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: 3270 emulator?

_(reply depth: 10)_

- **From:** Michael Wojcik <mwojcik@newsguy.com>
- **Date:** 2011-06-08T13:27:09-04:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<isonib18nm@news4.newsguy.com>`
- **In-Reply-To:** `<9559goFh4nU1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<94qhabFoamU1@mid.individual.net> <85373ac0-92bc-4e7c-b3ad-6d0595e21d22@s9g2000yqm.googlegroups.com> <ka3iu6516gnkgoo95itjnbmvvv51vdokt7@4ax.com> <cf1f4589-2841-488f-a126-42f132bf9f18@gh5g2000vbb.googlegroups.com> <94t37uF98iU1@mid.individual.net> <1g0ju6l4gimc5uu9n5l6igu4nuublehncn@4ax.com> <94thd7F8k9U1@mid.individual.net> <72599b39-ea2f-4e67-bb24-7142c060dcc3@j25g2000vbr.googlegroups.com> <isit12417mr@news7.newsguy.com> <9559goFh4nU1@mid.individual.net>`

```
Pete Dashwood wrote:
> Michael Wojcik wrote:
>>
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> That last sentence would be a good one to drop at a cocktail party... :-)

I've thrown it out in discussions at conferences recently. It's a
useful touchstone when people outside the field are discussing NLP and
you want to see who actually knows something of the theory. (NLP is
*big* in some of the humanities right now, at least in the US. The
National Science Foundation has a grant series for projects that apply
IT to large corpora of humanities data, such as digitized books.)

> Given your specialised interest in languiage Michael, is there any VR 
> software you would currently recommend, or do you think this technology 
> still has a way to go?

To be honest I don't know much about commercial speech recognition
software. I've mostly been dealing with text. Those problem domains
eventually converge, when you start doing semantic processing - ie,
trying to figure out meaning - but most of the speech-recognition work
these days that I've seen is in one of two areas: improving the
precision of recognizing relatively straightforward speech (this is
the "tell the robot what to do" stuff); or working on some of the
harder problems of speech environments, like conversation entailment
(when you have multiple speakers, figuring out who's speaking and how
their statements fit together into a conversation).

But as for recommendations, my feeling is that if it works for your
purposes, why not use it? Speech recognition works fairly well for
computer navigation, and some people (including some disabled users)
like to use it that way.

It works OK for some speech-to-text / transcription purposes,
especially if you don't mind editing the result; I know it's quite
popular for transcribing interviews, and medical and legal
transcription software is big business (because accuracy is a problem
and there's a lot of money in transcribing that data).

I've never found it to work at all well for writing code, partly
because in most languages punctuation is so important.

The Japanese-language teaching software I used was based on one of the
Dragon engines, and it worked quite well for its purpose.

If you want to write ordinary prose (ie, not specialized stuff like
medical or legal jargon) using speech-to-text, I suspect one of the
Dragon products would work pretty well.
```

Don't allow them to do both eyes in one session (it could make you
completely blind). The cornea can be easily damaged and trauma can
reduce oxygen transport across the cornea which could result in the
cornea going opaque.

Thanks for the kind thoughts, Kerry.

Christchurch is just a heartbreak and the people seem to be getting no 
respite.

It is hard for people not in contact with people there, to understand the 
devastating effect these constant shocks over months (with no sign of them 
abating, although most experts agree they will eventually) have on the 
morale and spirit of the people. Many have left, of course, but there are 
still many who see it as their home and don't want to leave. There are some 
who simply can't leave. Businesses are losing patience and if investment is 
pulled out the City will die. I am in contact with a friend there and I can 
tell that he is finding it hard, although he puts a brave face on it. We had 
200 people, mainly families, (drawn by lot) come to Tauranga (which is a 
seaside resort) for 10 days for some relief and they were suitably wined and 
dined and entertained by our City and the citizens of it. It was great to 
see and the kids set up twinning with local schools. To me, the most 
poignant part was seeing them have to go back...

This article gives some insight and statistics: 
http://www.scoop.co.nz/stories/HL1106/S00003/grim-statistics-and-worrying-predictions-100-days-on.htm

The population of NZ is a little over 4 million. $30 billion is a big ask...

Pete.

Thanks for your post, Charlie.

This disaster has made me re-evaluate a lot of things in my life.

We all generally do the best we can. I saw the tornadoes in the USA on TV 
and the people who had their homes and their lives destroyed overnight. The 
Japan earthquake where many thousands lost their lives. All of these natural 
disasters are things which are beyond our control and we try to come to 
terms with them the best way we can. Until it actually touches you and 
people you care about it all seems a bit remote; it is something that 
happens to other people. And then you realise that we are all "other people" 
and we all need to support each other if the world is to be worth living in.

Although I don't share your faith, I do appreciate your prayers and I don't 
know of any case where people praying did any harm...:-)

I am doing something here which I would never normally do and that is reveal 
some private mail. I know the person concerned won't mind and it may help 
others to get some insight into what things are like in Christchurch.

<quote from mail received from ChCh yesterday>

Hi Pete ---we had 2 very big earth quakes on Monday  -----very violent ---it 
was terrible --The house shook so badly I thought it would break up and fall 
apart and the roof would fall down upon us.---I had to keep preparing my 
lesson for Tuesday night .  I was shaking a bit ---but I delivered my lesson 
and it went OK,

Rhonda went away to our home town Ashburton to hide. --I don't blame her...

We have the builder here today trying to fix our front door so it can be 
opened and shut.
The house has suddenly sunk down and the door wont open or close.

Liquefaction ( Grey stinky mud) is oozing up from underground and covering 
our lawn and gardens and driveway and roadways ---it is horrible.

Scientists tell us there is a good chance that there will be another big 
quake very soon.

YUK!   We cant sleep because the next one might come any minute.

regards, <name removed>

</quote from mail received from ChCh yesterday>

As you can see this is simple and understated but quite chilling.

Pete.

- **From:** Michael Wojcik <mwojcik@newsguy.com>
- **Date:** 2011-06-06T11:24:07-04:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<isit12317mr@news7.newsguy.com>`
- **In-Reply-To:** `<94thd7F8k9U1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<94qhabFoamU1@mid.individual.net> <85373ac0-92bc-4e7c-b3ad-6d0595e21d22@s9g2000yqm.googlegroups.com> <ka3iu6516gnkgoo95itjnbmvvv51vdokt7@4ax.com> <cf1f4589-2841-488f-a126-42f132bf9f18@gh5g2000vbb.googlegroups.com> <94t37uF98iU1@mid.individual.net> <1g0ju6l4gimc5uu9n5l6igu4nuublehncn@4ax.com> <94thd7F8k9U1@mid.individual.net>`

```
Pete Dashwood wrote:
> 
> The first time I ever saw voice recognition was on a CDC Cyber mainframe in 
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
> eventually get 100%, or so close as doesn't matter.

The problems to be solved in getting that last 8% are more difficult
than the ones solved for the first 92%.

In particular, for generalized input, homophony is a killer. Humans
can't solve it with 100% accuracy - we frequently mishear other
speakers. And we have much, much, much better language recognition
capabilities than any machine system yet implemented.

In English, the homophone problem is relatively small, because English
is so irregular, and because we have additional cues like stress. Even
so general-purpose automated speech recognition, or ASR, systems (like
the ones people are experimenting with for home robotics - I saw a
paper on this last month) have a lot of trouble distinguishing words
with similar sounds, or even correctly parsing the audio stream into
distinct words. So many ASR systems are augmented with things like
gesture recognition.

In some other languages the homophone problem is much worse. Japanese,
for example, is loaded with homophones.

The depths of these problems are hard to grasp without reading some of
the research. For example, linguists who study prosody still aren't
sure what syllable stress *is* (it seems to sometimes be sound
pressure, sometimes timing, sometimes small sharp pitch inflections,
sometimes other things), which makes it hard to integrate it into your
acoustic model. And that's just at the tokenizing (and sometimes
word-disambiguation) stage. Then there's word identification, parsing,
sense disambiguation, application of world knowledge, coreference
resolution, predictive matching based on historical interaction,
application of sanity-checking and safety rules...

Then, when you start to build general-purpose UIs out of ASR, you run
into all sorts of other problems: incorrect user expectations, speaker
identification, addressee identification, increased environmental
noise, conversation entailment, domain identification, etc. Again,
even humans aren't close to 100% accurate with this stuff. (Remember
asking secretaries to read back dictation? And they were human
*experts* in this area.)

That 92% accuracy is the low-hanging fruit of ASR. While various users
have different tolerances for "close enough" to 100%, many will find
general-purpose ASR insufficient for their needs for the foreseeable
future, I believe.

What we will see, I suspect, are more and more limited-purpose ASR
systems, often augmented by things like gesture and facial-expression
recognition, and in some cases with strong domain cuing. (So, for
example, you'd tell your home robot that you need it to hand you tools
as you ask for them, putting it into "tool recognition mode" until you
gave it a different task. Or you'd put it into "home bartender mode".
The mode it's in would help it figure out what you want when you ask
for a screwdriver.)
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: 3270 emulator?

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>

```
Howard Brazee wrote:
> On Sat, 4 Jun 2011 09:49:49 +1200, "Pete Dashwood"
> <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
> have to disagree with you, old dogs and new tricks you know.

I've used voice input, and I find it terrible for nearly everything I
do with computers.

The error rate's too high, and homophony makes that an extremely
difficult problem to solve (within a tolerance I'd find adequate) in
the general case. The entry rate is slow - slower than touch-typing,
for anything other than conversation. It's intrusive and
space-inefficient if you have multiple users, or even a single user
and multiple devices, if it's done with normal vocalization (an
inherently broadcast medium); you can direct it with microphones, but
then you lose some of the purported advantages (like omnidirectionality).

I've looked at a great many input devices, and for trained users
writing in small-alphabet languages, I've yet to find one that equals
(much less bests) the keyboard. And writing in small-alphabet
languages is 95% of what I do with a general-purpose computer.

For special-purpose devices, either I mostly write with them (eg phone
texting) or my interactions are so fleeting that there's no advantage
to "improving" the UI. I don't need to be able to tell my car stereo
to switch songs, for example; a button does that just fine.
```

>

I only encountered two compiler bugs in my almost 40 years of COBOL 
programming.  My first compile on a CDC 6400 computer sent the compiler into 
a loop due to a missing period.  The second one had to do with referencing a 
field that was defined but that the compiler seemed to think was not 
defined.  It was very hard to determine the cause because inserting DISPLAY 
staetments would cause it to compile correctly.  Finally I was able to 
reproduce it  with DISPALY statements in otherr places and I got around it 
by simply placing a paragraph name in the code along with a comment to never 
remove that paragraph name.

```
On 6/4/2011 12:16 AM, Pete Dashwood wrote:
> "Charles Hottel"<chottel@earthlink.net>  wrote in message
> news:1I2dnZd5iqyRA3TQnZ2dnUVZ_g2dnZ2d@earthlink.com...
…[24 more quoted lines elided]…
> Pete.

Back in 1979, when I had been a professional COBOL programmer less than 
six months, my project leader and I found a bug in the ANSI-74 COBOL 
compiler for an IBM Series/1 minicomputer.  The problem was in the 
DIVIDE GIVING REMAINDER statement, which we were using in a day-of-week 
calculation.  If you divided 9 by 7 the answer was 1 with a remainder of 
2, but if you divided 2 by 7 the remainder was zero instead of 2.  So it 
rarely caused a problem, and we could code around it.  We filled out an 
IBM APAR (Authorized Program Analysis Report), and eventually IBM send 
us a patch for that compiler bug.

My project leader once compiled a program that caused the compiler to 
crash, with no diagnostics.  He kept cutting the program in half until 
he found the statement in the program that caused the compiler to fail. 
  I think it turned out to be something like a missing period that 
caused a flood of error messages which overflowed an intermediate work 
file.  We managed to work around that problem too.

Those were the good old days!

And to keep this on topic, I'm pretty sure that Windows NT 4 included a 
Microsoft branded 3270 emulator.

Kind regards,
```

Not much of a welcome to COBOL :-)

I'm really surprised to hear this Arnold. I worked for CDC in the early '70s 
when they were trying to transition from being a "Scientific" company into 
supporting Commerce as well. I know that a lot of money was spent on their 
COBOL tools (including the compiler). I wrote a tool for them called COSSET 
(COBOL source edit and translate) which enabled other COBOL to be easily 
migrated to CDC. It isn't surprising that a bug was encountered (compilers 
are complex pieces of software), but rather that it was such a simple thing. 
As noted elsewhere, I never encountered a COBOL bug in that environment, but 
I was working entirely on Cyber (although the COBOL toolset was inherited 
from 6400) and some minisystems aimed mainly at process control. It is 
possible that the bug was fixed fairly quickly.

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: 3270 emulator?

_(reply depth: 6)_

- **From:** "Charles Hottel" <chottel@earthlink.net>
- **Date:** 2011-06-04T12:18:55-04:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<97ednQg_D7ZDx3fQnZ2dnUVZ_u2dnZ2d@earthlink.com>`
- **References:** `<94qhabFoamU1@mid.individual.net> <85373ac0-92bc-4e7c-b3ad-6d0595e21d22@s9g2000yqm.googlegroups.com> <ka3iu6516gnkgoo95itjnbmvvv51vdokt7@4ax.com> <94telmFmmjU1@mid.individual.net> <1I2dnZd5iqyRA3TQnZ2dnUVZ_g2dnZ2d@earthlink.com> <94ttd7Fku4U1@mid.individual.net>`

```

"Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote in message 
news:94ttd7Fku4U1@mid.individual.net...
>
>
…[29 more quoted lines elided]…
>

I must have been tired last night.  Anyway I woke up this morning and 
remembered clearly that the second bug that I mentioned was an IF statement 
that always took the ELSE even when the condition was true.  This bug was 
not on CDC6400 but was on IBM equipment.

Yes the CDC6400 used Compass and its job control language was great. 
Instead of coding a DD statement for a disk file you just did a REWIND 
DISK01.  It had a great editor.  When adding and changing source code you 
could associate a unique identifier with all of the changes and you could 
use it later to remove the changes if you wanted to.  We had report programs 
that accepted control cards that had many options.  Instead of making the 
users code the control cards I coded up all the possibilities and associated 
them with keywords that were meaningful to the users.  They entered the 
keywords and the editor selected the appropriate parameter cards.  The users 
loved it.
```

#### ↳ Re: 3270 emulator?

- **From:** Richard <riplin@Azonic.co.nz>

```
Charles Hottel wrote:
> "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote in message 
> news:94tg1dFvjsU1@mid.individual.net...
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> The basic way that the Windows GUI works has not changed much,

>From Windows 3.1? It's changed pretty substantially. Perhaps you've
forgotten just what 3.1 was like.

> however from
> different versions of MS Office programs options from drop-down menus have 
> sometimes moved from one menu to a nother menu and this requies some 
> retraining/unlearning. 

One important issue here (well, important to the question - the
importance of the question I'll leave aside) is what Richard and Pete
mean by "retraining". I suspect Richard means it in a cognitive sense,
which would include learning the new interfaces without any formal, or
necessarily even conscious, process; while Pete means it in the
pedagogical sense.

Many of the system applications, tools, and basic UI functions have
changed significantly from Win 3.1 to Win 7. For example, Win 3.1 File
Manager and Program Manager - the two most basic UI components - were
combined into Explorer, which now runs in two modes, as the desktop
(including the Start button and Task Bar) and as individual Explorer
(and the redundant Folder View) windows. That's a sweeping change to
appearance, a major one to UI "feel", and a large one to affordances.

Ergo, users moving from one to the other had to learn new controls.
```

computer and your phone are on evolutionary strands which are converging. (I 
saw a comedian the other day bewailing the fact that his new phone can't 
call people but it tells him the phases of the moon. The audience laughed, 
so there were people who related to it.) The boundaries between phone and 
computer are already being blurred; no such blurring exists between car and 
TV or bike and washing machine.

is actually a pretty good OS. I seem to recall that most people didn't want

On Jun 4, 6:30 pm, "Pete Dashwood"
<dashw...@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:
> "Richard" <rip...@Azonic.co.nz> wrote in message
>
…[33 more quoted lines elided]…

It was user retraining that was referred to, as in: "how to use all

API, which itself has changed from Windows, to Win32, to .NET, and now

Some friends of mine have a seven year old girl and she was set an

Zealand. (We have two major political parties here, National and Labour, 
roughly corresponding to Republicans and Democrats).

Jasmine and her mother sat down to go through the 40 questions. One of them 
was: "What are the 2 major parties in New Zealand?"

dawns... "Christmas and New Year!"

For a long time some fathers of a puritanical nature (and others with a 
mischevious sense of humour)  tried to frighten their sons out of 
masturbation by telling them it would make them go blind.

outside occasionally...

staring at screens and not for any other reason... :-)

May I respectfully suggest that in 10 years time, if I asked you whether you

I don't believe you remained with green screens any more than you did with 
punched cards.

I recall a programmer showing me the new 3270 screens when they first came 
out. The fact that text could have characters inserted and removed by 
placing a cursor  in the line was considered amazing. (previously a whole 
card had to be repunched if you made an error). I believe the new surface 
technologies are going to be a bit like that, but for the general public, 
rather than specialist programmers.

As always, time will tell :-)

Pete.

#### ↳ Re: 3270 emulator?

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2011-06-08T10:41:31+12:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<957notFl6hU1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<94qhabFoamU1@mid.individual.net>`

```
Pete Dashwood wrote:
> COBOLlers concerned with improving the user experience (if there are
> any) might be interested in this...
…[12 more quoted lines elided]…
> Pete.

Although this was posted with tongue-in-cheek it does appear that we are at 
a significant point in the development of how people interact with 
computers.

I have just been reading a preview of the new Mac OSX Lion and iOS 5, and 
surface technologies play a big part in them.

Features of the new Apple offerings are:

1. Mac OS X Lion is the 8th major release of the OS.

2. It will be available in July.

3. It has over 250 new features (not all of which were detailed)

4. It comes with 3,000 new developer APIs.

5. It will be only available by download through the Mac App Store.

NOTE: 6. It features Multi-Touch gestures and fluid animations that let you 
interact directly with content on the screen

NOTE: 7. New gestures include momentum scrolling, tapping or pinching your 
fingers to zoom in on a web page or image, and swiping left or right to turn 
a page or switch between full screen apps.

(Points 6 and 7 were demonstrated in the Win 8 video link previously posted. 
The point is that this is NOT a particular vendor's technology; it is across 
the board.)

8. All Mac notebooks ship with Multi-Touch trackpads and desktop Macs can 
use Apple's Magic Trackpad.

9. Mac OS X Lion features full screen apps to take advantage of the entire 
display

10. The Mission Control feature combines Expos�, full screen apps, Dashboard 
and Spaces.

11. You can swipe to get your desktop to zoom out to display your open 
windows grouped by app, thumbnails of your full screen apps and your 
Dashboard.

(These "thumbnails" correspond to "tiles" in the Win 8 video).

12. It comes with the Mac App Store built in.

13. The Launchpad feature makes it easier to find and launch apps, and with 
a single multi-touch gesture, all your apps are displayed in a full screen 
layout.

14. You can organize apps into any order or into folders and swipe through 
unlimited pages of apps.

15. There's a redesigned Mail app with widescreen layout.

16. The Mail app has a new conversations feature that groups related 
messages into a scrollable timeline.

17. The Mail app has a new search feature that lets you refine searches and 
suggests matches by person, subject, and label as you type.

18. The mail app also includes support for Microsoft Exchange 2010.

19. The OS includes Resume, which lets you bring apps back to how you left 
them when you restart or relaunch the app.

20. Apps will save automatically as you work.

21. Versions records the history of your documents as you create them, and 
gives you a way to browse, revert, and copy/paste from previous versions.

22. An AirDrop feature lets you find nearby Macs and set up P2P wireless 
connections.

23. Mac OS X Lion will be available as an upgrade to Snow Leopard from the 
Mac App Store for $29.99.

24. The upgrade will be at about 4GB (the size of an HD movie from the 
iTunes Store).

iOS 5:


25. Apple calls iOS 5 "the most extensive software update ever for iPhone, 
iPad, and iPod Touch."

26. iOS 5 has over 200 new features.

27. iOS 5 is a free update.

28. iOS 5 gives devices access to iCloud (see below)

29. Apple has not detailed all 200 features, but highlights a few as "key 
features," which include: notification center, iMessage, Newsstand, 
Reminders, Twitter, Camera, Photos, Safari, and PC Free.

30. Notification Center aggregates all of your notifications, and can be 
accessed simply by swiping down from the top of the screen.

31. It shows stocks and weather at the top of the screen.

32. It will display a little pop up at the top to let you know about it 
while you're watching a video or playing a game, then it will go away.

33. The "Newsstand" feature automatically downloads (in the background) the 
latest issues of magazine and newspaper apps that you're subscribed to, and 
keeps the available for online or offline access from one place.

(There were 114 points in the article which I received through email so 
cannot post a link.)

It may be that the mouse is becoming redundant.

Usually it takes a few years for what people are using at home to appear in 
the Enterprise. As familiarity with the new interfaces is gained, pressure 
to have them in the workplace mounts. Many enterprise applications are being 
outsourced or replaced by packages and the vendors want their software to 
have maximum appeal. They therefore implement what are generally accepted 
interfaces.

The days when enterprise software was developed in-house by a COBOL shop, 
and that shop dictated to the business how the interface would work, are 
pretty much over. Even places that still develop their own in-house software 
are now much more responsive to what the business wants. If the business is 
not satisfied with the service they get from IT they have other options (for 
many years they never did.)

Workers coming in to industry today are very much more "computer literate" 
than they were 30 years ago. They are not interested in the technicalities; 
if they can do it on a cell phone they expect to be able to do it on a 
computer, and that includes computers in the workplace.

I'll stick my neck out here and say that by 2015 (my predicted date for the 
demise of COBOL as a major development language, although this has already 
happened) we will be seeing the interface technology described above (and 
demonstrated in the Win 8 video link which I posted) appearing in enterprise 
applications.

I believe this will be driven by familiarity with surface technology gained 
on home computers and cell phones.

As always, time will tell :-)

Pete.
```

- **Date:** 2011-06-10T00:31:39+02:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<c05093d84a49f2aef644bb3c968c31c3@msgid.frell.theremailer.net>`

```
"Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:

> Thanks Fritz.
> 
> Food for thought in that.
> 
> Pete

Hi Pete. I should have said this is my experience as somebody who writes
software for that environment. Our experience may not be typical since we
need to stay ahead of the curve somewhat and tend to get new machines right
about every 2 years. I am not real current on what production shops are
doing. It doesn't bother our sysprogs too much since they are usually
running early release OS and program products and don't have production
other than keeping the developers working. I do know some of our clients run
very old unsupported hardware software combos, even big clients who you
wouldn't think would take the chance. The way they look at things is right
now we have a running production system serving x million customers. We
would rather stay with what we know works than spend x million dollars and
upgrade to the latest stuff even though ours went off support because in the
long run it might cost us more. And they are big enough when push comes to
shove they will get IBM to fix critical problems in their hardware and
software. Other customers deliberately stay a behind just because they can
get hardware coming off lease much cheaper but they are also taking a
chance. I also know of some clients who have ancient homegrown systems they
can't afford to upgrade. Things are expensive and business is tight and to
support their customizations on the latest hardware and software would be
cost prohibitive. So they lease a current or -1 system and they own their
ancient system (paid off years ago) and use both for production. Things vary
alot but as far as what you asked, I hope I gave accurate info. You could
check on IBM-MAIN but you'll have to sign up for it. There is a usenet
mirror but nobody on the mailing list sees it. That is where most of the
remaining sysprogs hang out. They can give you more details.
```

Fritz Wuehler wrote:
> "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:

Thanks again for the clarification, Fritz.

I do take a look at IBM-MAIN from time to time, using the Usenet mirror you 
mentioned.

It is kind of sad that people can't risk updating their software because 
their investment is too huge to take a chance, and then, as you noted, 
others take a chance that they really shouldn't have to take.

As for times being tough, I think that is across the board and across the 
World. PRIMA is surviving at the moment on reserves I set aside for it some 
time ago; although traffic to the site is steady and people are looking at 
the Migration Toolset there has been a decline in people actually getting 
it. It may be that the time to Migrate COBOL is over, it may be that moving 
from a procedural to an OOP environment is just too hard conceptually. Or it 
just may be that things are too uncertain for people to spend money at the 
moment.

I've been disappointed by the number of people who have viewed the Data 
Migration 5 cent tour (it is still under 100) and it is hard to know whether 
this is because people have trouble seeing it (I used HTML5 but I'm thinking 
Silverlight might have been better...) or because they aren't interested.

Here's a link: http://primacomputing.co.nz/COBOL21/demosandtutorials.aspx

(The 5 cent tour is around 15 minutes... maybe this is too long? I couldn't 
really do it any quicker :-))

As a result, I haven't proceeded to do the Code Conversion videos which 
really are more exciting (at least to COBOL programmers) because they show 
the Toolset rewriting existing COBOL legacy code completely automatically, 
to use Data Access Layer objects so it can access the RDBMS, instead of 
Indexed files. It automates the task of turning procedural code into Object 
Oriented code, and this is useful as an example for programmers interested 
in doing this. (Video 1 covers why you would need to do this, with 
background concepts and arguments.)

I'll happily discuss here any of the concepts as to why we take the approach 
we have taken.

Pete.
```

been promoted to Senior Executive Manager, West Coast Division, and you're 
not allowed to get a new car, ever... and if you're late for work the 
company collapses.'

continuation of your making money (I'm assuming that's the goal of most 
organisations) then either you have an unique product/process which can be 
left alone or you need to be putting time, money and effort in improving 
things... or else your competition will.

"Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:

> It is kind of sad that people can't risk updating their software because 
> their investment is too huge to take a chance, and then, as you noted, 
> others take a chance that they really shouldn't have to take.

I don't think it's sad. It might be bad planning, it might be a good or bad
business decision for them, who knows? I don't think running after the
latest hardware and software is something that ever ends so if you can avoid
to some degree, you should. Some of the most backlevel customers are big
banks and financial services providers who can't mess around with other
peoples money. (the execs seem to, just not the data center staff ;-))

I've never heard of PRIMA but I take it from what you wrote here it's your
company. I wish you all the best, everyone's having a hard time now.

> it. It may be that the time to Migrate COBOL is over, it may be that
> moving from a procedural to an OOP environment is just too hard
> conceptually. Or it just may be that things are too uncertain for people
> to spend money at the moment.

I personally could never budget for any kind of migration of existing
code. I think it's money wasted. OOP hasn't been proven in many kinds of
back end processing or rather it's been proven not to be applicable. Where
new technologies are worth pursuing they ought to be pursued for new
development. It's not cost effective to convert old code because you can't
afford to convert the old coders with it. I consider people more important
than business at the end of the day and if can get the job done with old
code and old people I prefer that and will choose it over firing the old
people and hiring new people to write new code. Ideally we could get the old
coders to write the new code and often we do. There are a few fairly big
companies doing "application modernization" "rehosting" etc. so I imagine in
that niche the competition is sharp and they prefer a big outfit. I've seen
several projects where hundreds of millions of dollars were wasted. Wasted
as in the first years of the project were a total loss and additional years
had to be budgeted to do what should have been done in the first place,
sometimes by removing the first contractor and hiring a second or
third. Doing big systems right is a very difficult job and most companies
and the people they hire just aren't qualified. If you can distinguish
yourself with a few big jobs done right and within budget that will instill
confidence.
```

program and generate the ProtSyms for Intertest, start working, find out 
that my UserID is allowed access to *some* CICS commands but not others

he began to raise his voice.  Forunately, someone on the conference-call

unspoken, as the conference-call guy broke in - was 'I did all this 
because I am a fool, a dolt, a chump and an incompetent janitor who 
masquerades as a coder with decades' worth of experience... and you got 
fooled into hiring me so why should you believe I'm doing anything that's

I am not sure how you are distinguishing between 'you are' used as a
singular and 'you are' used as a plural. 'you' is both singular 2nd
person and plural. 'are' is more frequently used by plurals (as in 'he
is', 'they are').

- **Date:** 2011-06-13T01:54:55-07:00

- **References:** `<95d3n1FgeU1@mid.individual.net> <1db04a8d06cef571a221e91a3f21e641@dizum.com> <isu9er$t0r$3@reader1.panix.com> <95ih9dFf4U1@mid.individual.net>`

```
On Jun 11, 7:58 pm, "Pete Dashwood"
<dashw...@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:
> docdw...@panix.com wrote:
> > From my experience there's a bit of the World o' Data Processing
…[10 more quoted lines elided]…
> though.


While the original 1401 emulation had significant hardware components
in S/360, it lost that over the years.  I think a couple of the low
end S/370s had some hardware assist, but nothing after that.  The
1401/1440/1460 emulator was still available for DOS/VSE on the 4341,
which had *no* 14xx support in hardware, and by that time the
emulators also had no particular OS support (IOW, they were just
ordinary programs).  FWIW, I verified that by pulling an ancient "A
Guide to the IBM 4341 Processor" from the archives, which had all of
"Section 40" devoted to the 14xx emulators.

Anyway, the 4341 was obviously not much intended for MVS (OS/VS2), but
I think the OS versions of the emulators followed a similar course.
There would be a good chance that those would still run on zOS (and a
decent chance that the DOS/VSE emulators would still run on zVSE),
although finding I/O devices would be a problem (at least teh DOS/VSE
emulators did most of their disk and tape I/O at the EXCP level, and I
don't know if they every supported anything more recent than 3350 and
3370), and 9-track tape drives are going to be tough to find too.
```

```
On Jun 13, 3:17 pm, Clark F Morris <cfmpub...@ns.sympatico.ca> wrote:
> On Mon, 13 Jun 2011 01:54:55 -0700 (PDT), "robertwess...@yahoo.com"
>
…[44 more quoted lines elided]…
> 360/65 got drenched from a leak in the ceiling.


No question - there were a fair number of (small) MVS installation on
4341s.  But until some of the faster models with more memory came out
(the first models were limited to 4MB), it was a pretty tight fit, and
you didn't see much MVS (and I'm specifically referring to the OS/VS2
branch of the OS/360 line) in the early days.  The early doc for the
4341 didn't reference MVS (or OS/VS2) at all, although they did
explicitly support OS/VS1 (there was even an ECPS mode for that).  See
the announcement letter for the 4331 & 4341 from 1979:

http://www-03.ibm.com/ibm/history/exhibits/mainframe/mainframe_PP4341.html
```

Nomen Nescio wrote:
> "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:
>
…[8 more quoted lines elided]…
> if you can avoid to some degree, you should.

Fair comment.

It's sad if they would like to move onward but can't.


>Some of the most
> backlevel customers are big banks and financial services providers
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
> now.

Thanks. :-)

I will attend to it as soon as my batteries are recharged... :-)

>
>> it. It may be that the time to Migrate COBOL is over, it may be that
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
> code. I think it's money wasted.

It certainly can be. It is nice to have an option though and sometimes the 
existing code is really running perfectly well and has done for a very long 
time. Our tools allow people to lever this code into a networked 
environment. It is achieved by converting all indexed data files to a 
relational database (or databases), generating objects which manage this new 
database, then changing the existing codebase to utilize these objects. New 
code being developed can use the same objects (although it doesn't HAVE to) 
so the new stuff can share the same data as the old, without the need to 
synchronize the new DB with legacy files every night. I does mean that 
existing procedural code gets re-factored or re-wrapped so it can utilize an 
OO environment. The new systems can be written in OO COBOL (although I don't 
personally recommend this - after using it for 10 years I came to realize 
that the OO languages are simply better for OO programming...) or any of the 
OO languages and everything plays nicely together on the level playing 
fierld which is .Net.

> OOP hasn't been proven in many kinds
> of back end processing or rather it's been proven not to be
> applicable.

Agreed. However the whole question of whether a back end system can be 
justified in a modern environment needs revisiting.

Just because we currently have back-end systems doesn't mean we will always 
need such systems and many processes like report consolidation can run as 
separate threads in a multiprocessed environment. The role of back-end 
processing in support of online processing (completing transactions which 
would take too long to do in real time) has pretty much been subsumed by the 
development of networked applications, where a transaction can definitely 
complete in real time and can dynamically add more processors and threads as 
required in order to do so.(Update threads can be instanced as objects, as 
and when they are required for example...)

In the networked environment, Object Orientation is pretty much essential, 
and more and more businesses are recognising the advantages of networked 
solutions, moving their applications to be Web enabled or re-wrapped as Web 
Services in a SOA. To be able to bring legacy code into this environment is 
something which MAY be of great value. Other people will decide to redevelop 
for the new environment and simply write off the legacy code or run it in 
parallel until it has been replaced. Obviously, what people decide to do 
depends on their specific circumstances, but our tools offer the option of 
bringing legacy code painlessly into a modern environment, and it is always 
good to have options.


> Where new technologies are worth pursuing they ought to
> be pursued for new development. It's not cost effective to convert
> old code because you can't afford to convert the old coders with it.

That's why we provide tools to do it automatically. It is very cost 
effective if you can do quickly and easily.

> I consider people more important than business at the end of the day
> and if can get the job done with old code and old people I prefer
> that and will choose it over firing the old people and hiring new
> people to write new code.

I endorse those sentiments. However, I don't believe it is necessary to be 
locked in to old code in order to effect it. And you can run old and new 
code seamlessly together if you choose our solution.



> Ideally we could get the old coders to
> write the new code and often we do. There are a few fairly big
> companies doing "application modernization" "rehosting" etc. so I
> imagine in that niche the competition is sharp and they prefer a big
> outfit.

Yes, you'd think so. However, we have done and assisted a number of very 
successful migrations and they chose us because our toolset is unique, we 
have a vested interest in seeing customers succeed (and make sure they 
do...), and the price for our tools is less than the price for a new .Net 
COBOL compiler. I think part of the success has been because we simply 
aren't big enough to handle multiple migrations simultaneously. That means 
we stay focussed with each client until Migration is complete.  (I suppose 
we could handle several small ones simultaneously, but I wouldn't want to be 
too dispersed if we had a customer doing a major migration.)

>I've seen several projects where hundreds of millions of
> dollars were wasted.

Now, if they had done that with us, they would have only wasted a few 
hundred thousand... :-)

Seriously, we have never had a failed Migration or a dissatisfied customer. 
Knowledge has been acquired and reapplied on every project. PRIMA is now a 
centre of competence for leveraging legacy COBOL into the .Net environment. 
The Toolset today reflects this experience and is a far cry from the 
original I wrote nearly 10 years ago.

>Wasted as in the first years of the project were
> a total loss and additional years had to be budgeted to do what
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
> that will instill confidence.

Well, we have one or two that were "major" (Though not on the scale you 
mentioned above.) There is no shortage of referrals and I really enjoy 
reading the unsolicited testimonials we have received. I have never exceeded 
a quoted price, and we frequently add value which we don't charge for. (I 
consider it an investment in goodwill and in making the Toolset better; also 
the overheads for us are low and that helps.) I am really interested in 
Small to Medium Enterprises (because PRIMA is such a company) where you can 
get things done without having huge amounts of red tape and waiting for 
committees to approve action.

Thanks for your comments and encouragement.

Please have a look around http://primacomputing.co.nz  for more information.

Pete.

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: 3270 emulator?

_(reply depth: 12)_

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2011-06-18T15:34:10+12:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<962kljFqisU1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<95hc8fFje9U1@mid.individual.net> <7907378e332a6ed768a22980486bb3ff@msgid.frell.theremailer.net>`

```
Fritz Wuehler wrote:
> "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:
>
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> have several emotions but sad isn't one of them!

I feel sad because of the waste.

And, as an IT professional, I don't like to see companies locked in to 
solutions for purely technical reasons.

Sure, sometimes bad  business (and IT ) decisions are made and there are 
consequences, but sometimes those bad decisions could have been easily 
avoided and that is sad.
>
>> Agreed. However the whole question of whether a back end system can
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
> systems.

Neither do I. That's why you get a "team" rather than a single aspect of the 
business to do it. (See below)


> In principle your statement is a good one, everything should
> be up for consideration whenever you have a new system to design, but
> there is also the idea of using what works and not spending
> unproductive time trying other angles that turn into blind alleys or
> have other issues associated with them.

But if you extend that argument you would never use anything new and would 
therefore miss out on the benefits associated with it. :-)

Sometimes there is a risk, but the the risk is usually calcuable, and the 
rewards can be balanced against it.

I firmly believe that application requirements (now, and as far as 
foreseeable, in the future) should drive system design. It should not be 
driven by things like: "We are a COBOL shop and you can't do that in 
COBOL..." (just as an example). The technology is not important, beyond the 
fact that you have certain databases already existing and these will be 
utilised by whichever technical approach you decide to adopt.

The trouble with getting programmers to design applications is that they 
design with a view to what they know technically.

(How many times in your career have you been told by a programmer: "That 
cannot be done." when what he really means is: "I don't know how to do 
that.")  It alwaysamuses me when you show them the solution and they then 
say: "Aw, well, if you're gonna do it like THAT..." :-)

The good thing about OOD is that you can do it on paper or a whiteboard and 
make "paper models" of processes before you even think about coding them, 
(they are called "use cases") and the language you select (Java, VB.Net, C#) 
is really pretty irrelevant. (In fact, sometimes the final system will 
consist of components developed in different languages (even OO COBOL), 
because it is the functionality (attributes, methods and events) of an 
object that is important and not the language it is coded in.)

I like Joint Application Development (JAD) workshops where users, analysts 
and programmers can sit down together and look at what needs to be achieved. 
Decide the use cases for the process and derive the objects that will be 
needed  to support those use cases. Then walk through them and be sure that 
they work and are viable in the business. (With an OO approach you don't 
have to have code to do that). Only then does code get cut and it is a 
process of building it, knowing that it is a first cut and will be changed. 
The tech people (usually programmers) present what they have to the JAD and 
everyone agrees on it or agrees it needs changing. If the changes are major 
(and they seldom are because major design flaws would have been caught at 
the "paper model" stage) then another JAD workshop is scheduled for later 
after the programmers have had a chance to make the necessary changes. If 
the changes are minor, they can be made on the spot.  The process is 
iterative (it gets repeated at least twice and usally three times) and 
interactive (changes are made on the spot) in line with the general 
principles of "agile" development. The results are that the business gets 
what it needs right now, rather than being locked in to what was identified 
during "requirements gathering" months ago. If requirements change during 
iteration of the development process, it is no problem. (In fact, it is 
pretty normal for them to do so.)

Compare this with the strict division of labour in a traditional approach. 
"I wrote what was in the spec. It's not my fault if it's wrong." (I once 
fired a programmer for saying that to me. He knowingly and deliberately 
coded something he knew full well was wrong, and he did it out of spite 
because he had some personal issues with the analyst who wrote the spec. He 
also believed that, as he had done what he was supposed to, there could be 
no repercussions. He was wrong. He got the union onto us and I had a session 
with them as well. They agreed I had the right to fire him, and I exercised 
it.)

I like JAD development for the reason that it keeps both technical and 
functional sides of the development engaged. Users see their system evolving 
and are part of it. When the release date comes they feel they own it, 
rather than having it thrust upon them by the IT department.


>That's a trade off but I like
> to go with what works.

Yep, me too. I like agile style development because I can see if it works 
BEFORE I build it.

> Saying that I have not written application
> code in several decades so the only real input I have is from
…[9 more quoted lines elided]…
> the term "back end processing".

Yes, I did. Sorry, I realise it can mean much more than that. Perhaps "Back 
Office processing " is better...


> I really didn't mean that although
> nightly processing is still a way of life in businesses like banks
> and insurance companies and financial services providers (even global
> ones) that do *most* of their processing during fixed hours.

That is the popular perception. (Certainly in this group.)  However, if you 
investigate it a bit it isn't what you expect. American Express, for 
example, a major credit card transaction processor started moving off 
"traditional" mainframe processing years ago and that process is continuing. 
The people I bank with (a major Australasian banking group) lost their 
mainframes years ago and they have been providing paperless statements  and 
online banking services for around 15 years now. (And they process their 
transactions without a mainframe.)

Sure, there are still some corporations who are happy with big iron but 
there are many who are moving to the network instead.

Try searching for IT jobs in Banking, Finance, Insurance, all the things you 
mention, and see the skill sets they are looking for. This is an indication 
of the equipment they are running. There isn't much COBOL.

Actually, it really doesn't matter whether you are right or I am on this. 
For COBOL programmers the future is bleak. 30 years ago, it was the only 
game in town; the town has moved on and found other games.

> What I
> really mean to say is processing that is about moving money,
> executing transactions, etc. There, performance and reliability and
> serviceability, all related somewhat to lines of code and controlling
> complexity play an issue.

There seems to be a popular misconception that only mainframes can process 
transactions securely. NETWORKS can also provide performance, reliability, 
and serviceabilty, not to mention cost effective scalability, and locality. 
They don't have to be taken down for "preventive maintenance" they can 
automatically bring more resources online as required (load levelling) and 
they can be fail safe. (The whole point of ARPANET, from which the internet 
developed, was that a nuclear strike could not bring it down, because the 
system would find alternative pathways to get around destroyed servers...)

If we examine your very interesting last sentence regarding lines of code 
and complexity needing to be minimised, COBOL loses again. Object systems 
typically contain far fewer lines of code than the same system developed in 
COBOL. A while back, just to satisfy my own curiosity, I did some analysis 
of my own codebase before and after COBOL. The COBOL solutions typically 
required anything from 1.5 to 6 times the coding of their replacements 
written in C#. This is not a definitive scientific analysis with proper 
controls in place but it was enough to persuade me that writing C# is a lot 
less effort than writing COBOL and the code produced is much more powerful. 
(Powerful in the sense that it can activate pre-written classes in a single 
line to do things that would take thousands of lines in COBOL. Maybe not a 
fair comparison but we are talking about minimising the code YOU WRITE.)

> And in those situations people are still
> using COBOL and even assembler to do those things and I don't see any
> move away from it even at shops that have spiffy Java apps for the
> front end.

I agree that some people are still using COBOL :-)

Perhaps you have noticed it is a diminishing number.

I'm not anti-COBOL (quite the contrary; it gave me an excellent living for 
many years and is still doing so to a much lesser extent. I am a strong 
advocate of bringing COBOL code into the 21st century (where it is sensible 
to do so) rather than discarding it. But it needs to be encapsulated so it 
can integrate properly and so it doesn't need lots of maintenance.)

The problem with COBOL is not actually the language, it is the paradigm it 
addresses. With a few exceptions that paradigm is not relevant to modern 
computer based commercial systems and there is an irony in that COBOL, 
originally developed for commercial data processing, is not relevant to it 
any more. Commerce changed (it became global) and the needs for data in 
organizations changed. The people needing the data also changed inasmuch as 
they are more enlightened and computer literate than they once were. They 
expect more than COBOL can easily deliver, and they are qute capable of 
processing their own data with spreadsheets and desktop databases.

>
>> The role of back-end processing in support of online processing
…[10 more quoted lines elided]…
> in real time.

No, my comment was really historical. Today's processors and data subsytems 
can usually manage it, but in the early days of the first online systems 
(mid to late 1970s), it was common practice for transaction updates to be 
deferred to overnight batch so that the user interface could be maintained 
in real time. There was no network and a centralised mainframe had to deal 
with it all.


> Some of them process multiple millions of transactions
> per hour. That's the strength of the mainframe and transaction
> processing systems like CICS and IMS.
>
Having worked with both CICS and IMS in the early days I agree they are 
robust transaction processing systems. But even then, there were better TP 
monitor and transaction processors available. (TASKMASTER and SHADOW were 
two that I used and found better. TASKMASTER was simply superb, but it 
didn't have a blue label on it and the peope who wrote it had no idea about 
how to market it. (cf. VHS and Betamax for videotape...)

There is still no comparison between a CICS based transactional system and a 
properly networked one using top end servers, today. Until you have seen and 
used both try to keep an open mind :-). I run various web applications on a 
server farm in Florida. This is a good example of a modern network. For the 
last 5 years, it has NEVER been down. I access remote SQL server DBs on this 
system and it is as if they are locally connected to my system here in NZ... 
instantaneous. It is the most cost effective data processing I have ever 
seen in over 40 years in the IT industry. I have been toying with moving to 
the cloud but I have no reason to. People who haven't dealt with modern 
networks really still perceive them as if they were home based LANs. They 
are not.



>> That's why we provide tools to do it automatically. It is very cost
>> effective if you can do quickly and easily.
>
> Perhaps but then you've got new code to maintain, and who'll do it?

No Fritz, the code is generated. It doesn't need maintenance. Your existing 
(legacy) code base is modified automatically. Changes to applications are 
made into the new systems being developed, not into the legacy. If you DID 
have to change the legacy (and you obviously avoid doing that if at all 
possible... it is legacy and being phased out) you would make the changes 
then re-generate the affected table sets on the RDB and the DAL objects 
affected. Affected programs get recompiled to reflect the new data structure 
in their working-storage interface and that's it. It usually takes a few 
minutes to make a minor change but the process of changing legacy is 
generally avoided.

If you decide to keep running legacy for any period of time, whether you do 
it with our toolset or do it manually, you will still need people to 
maintain it. The idea is to let the legacy keep running with minimal changes 
being applied to it, until the new systems can replace it. The advantage of 
our approach is that both old and new systems can use the same database, so 
if something does need changing it can be done with the new technology and 
the toolset can reflect it back to the legacy.

>
>> I endorse those sentiments. However, I don't believe it is necessary
…[13 more quoted lines elided]…
> to say so!

I understand.

I have no problem with disagreement provided it is fair and civil. When I 
have time, I'm glad to have discussions like this because it makes me 
re-evaluate my own position and that is very often fruitful for future 
development (of software and of me, personally :-))
>
>> Please have a look around http://primacomputing.co.nz  for more
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
> in the post hoping you can generate some new business :-)

While I appreciate the thought (thanks :-)), as far as I know, no business 
for PRIMA has ever been generated by posts to this forum. The people here 
are generally "shop floor" and their interest is more technical than 
purchasing. To be honest, I'm not entirely sure where our customer base has 
come from. Some of it will be web searches finding our site and some of it 
is definitely by referral. There have also been people who I know personally 
(but no-one from this forum). At least some are people who found our free 
COBOL structure analysis tool on the COBOL21 site. (This is the Tool that 
Robert Wagner and I developed to show the process of converting procedural 
COBOL into an OO environment and adding a modern GUI. The tool itself is 
useful and I still use it when I am working with COBOL. At the time, some 
people here said it was pointless and unnecessary as the compiler listing 
could show field offsets in a record definition, but it has been downloaded 
by hundreds of people and it has generated interest in the COBOL21 site, 
which has led to some people buying our toolset. These are people who are 
not COBOL guys and would not frequent this forum. But they are having to 
deal with COBOL legacy and need tools to help them.

I wish I DID know what gets them going because things are VERY quiet at the 
moment :-)

Thanks for your response, Fritz.

Pete.
