```
"Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:

> Fritz Wuehler wrote:
> > "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
> I feel sad because of the waste.

Waste is an intrinsic part of big business. There's no way around it and
it's not necessarily bad. People still got paid for doing the wrong thing.
They learned and didn't make the mistake again or they didn't learn and got
fired or stayed on. Who knows. But in the end money changed hands, people
worked, etc. If that isn't economy in action I don't know what is.

> And, as an IT professional, I don't like to see companies locked in to 
> solutions for purely technical reasons.

I don't know of a better reason to stay locked in. Marketing?

> > In principle your statement is a good one, everything should
> > be up for consideration whenever you have a new system to design, but
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
> would therefore miss out on the benefits associated with it. :-) 

No, you let the other people try the new stuff and you carefully evaluate
what they got and lost by doing that. All of the largest accounts I know are
intentional and careful late adopters. They are in the business of managing
other peoples money and they don't take chances they can avoid. One of the
most important ways they do that is simply not to run after new technology
for the sake of new. If they can support their business running backlevel or
very backlevel software on backlevel hardware that's exactly what they
do, on purpose. It might take 2 or 3 years to upgrade their operations
because of how much testing and signoff is required. It might take 6 months
or a year to put a single new piece of code into production once it's been
written or purchased. Those companies make careful studies of the published
reports and they are in close contact with their hardware and software
vendors, many of us actually have offices at customer sites just like IBM
did in the past. It all depends on your risk model so I want to present
another side that people might not be familiar with.

> Sometimes there is a risk, but the the risk is usually calcuable, and the 
> rewards can be balanced against it.

The risk for a big operation is mostly incalculable and has some important
intangibles like name/quality recognition etc which is why they are so
risk averse. Some of them worry more about reputation and loss of future
revenue and client retention than they do about a failure today. They're
very skittish. Many of them know exactly how much every second of down time
costs them (and the numbers are so outrageous I won't even attempt to post
them here) and the money isn't even their biggest concern. Their reputation
is worth more than their money, and they don't want outages or loss of
service. At all. The way they make that happen is a careful balance of
running old code and hardware and a long chain of testing and code
promotion. IBM is making running old hardware harder now than they did in
years gone by. Some of these companies have the muscle to make IBM bend and
some don't.

> I firmly believe that application requirements (now, and as far as 
> foreseeable, in the future) should drive system design. It should not be 
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> utilised by whichever technical approach you decide to adopt.

Yes and no. There are other factors like what do we have today, how much
does it cost us in people, software, and hardware. How much will we have to
pay for different people, software, and hardware to upgrade to the next
thing, what will it buy us in terms of real dollars, how long will it take
to pay for itself, and most of all, what if it doesn't work? And those are
just the tip of the iceberg. I'm glad I don't make those decisions.

> The trouble with getting programmers to design applications is that they 
> design with a view to what they know technically.

And we cannot blame us for that ;-) Remember in the old days the programmers
actually sat down with the end-user and they designed the systems more or
less together? Now big companies just sell you what they have, never mind
what you want. That's a mistake, but that's the way it is. Even if they
develop in house, the guy writing the code in a big shop seldom meets the
end user. Some system analyst goes to the end user and brings back a broken
system to design since he never actually writes code. We can all agree
that's not the way to work but nobody listens.

> (How many times in your career have you been told by a programmer: "That 
> cannot be done." when what he really means is: "I don't know how to do 
> that.")  It alwaysamuses me when you show them the solution and they then 
> say: "Aw, well, if you're gonna do it like THAT..." :-)

I have not heard that but as I said I have been away from applications for
several decades. Almost all of what we do hasn't been done before so "it
can't be done" isn't in our vocabulary. "It can't be done by this Friday" is
something you might hear us say, though ;-)

I snipped it for brevity but I don't see any conflict between traditional
coding methods and tools with the approach you suggest (focus group like
approach) and we have done that at several sites I have worked with in the
past. It's a good method, but only when you don't have much of a portfolio.
At some point you have products and then the salesmen have to sell those.
Nobody wants to keep developing new products, it's risky expensive and not
guaranteed, while the products you do have just require enough effort by
salesmen and minimal upgrading to retain existing licenses and keep the
quotas filled on new licenses. Not necessarily the way I would work, but
this is the reality in big software companies. Either that or buy more
existing products from another company. New product development is one of
the most expensive parts of the business and most "software" companies don't
want to deal with that part.

> Yep, me too. I like agile style development because I can see if it works 
> BEFORE I build it.

We're working in different worlds because any talk about agile in the
companies we sell to gets us invited out the door. They want stable, and it
doesn't matter how old it is or how much it costs. It must never break or do
anything to reduce availability of critical systems.

> There seems to be a popular misconception that only mainframes can process 
> transactions securely. NETWORKS can also provide performance, reliability, 
> and serviceabilty, not to mention cost effective scalability, and
> locality. 

That is all theory and it hasn't been proven, if anything recent events show
quite the opposite. Cloud or network failures have no limits, they affect
much more work than on a controlled back end environment. We have seen major
failures lately of companies like Amazon for technical or hacking reasons. I
haven't seen a hack of a mainframe in 4 decades and I don't expect to see
one.

> They don't have to be taken down for "preventive maintenance" they can 
> automatically bring more resources online as required (load levelling) and 
> they can be fail safe. (The whole point of ARPANET, from which the internet 
> developed, was that a nuclear strike could not bring it down, because the 
> system would find alternative pathways to get around destroyed servers...)

That design hasn't been proven, nor have any of the failovers worked without
hitches and glitches. Network isn't a silver bullet just like OO isn't a
silver bullet. The only thing that seems to never fail is a good old mainframe!

> 
> If we examine your very interesting last sentence regarding lines of code 
…[10 more quoted lines elided]…
> fair comparison but we are talking about minimising the code YOU WRITE.)

I don't know what you're including, but since Java and C# use substantial
libraries, I think you need to factor those in to your calculations. And if
you don't include locally written classes it's clearly not an apples and
apples comparison. When it comes to actual lines of code doing commercial
processing I don't think COBOL can be beat, since it's so ideal for
reporting, financial calculations and rounding (despite that other thread).
All those things take quite a bit of effort in other languages. Think of all
the work picture fields save you, and the automatic conversion between
packed and binary and display fields etc. No other language does that as
easily, certainly not Java or C#. You don't have to cast fields or call
conversion routines in COBOL, you just MOVE one field to another, and the
compiler does the work once, instead of every time you process a transaction
in Java/C#.

> Perhaps you have noticed it is a diminishing number.

I really haven't, I have seen quite a few new technologies deployed in big
companies, again only for front end processing like GUI and customer facing
work. They still buy and deploy big (and I think very poorly written)
application systems in COBOL and waste/spend hundreds of millions on it. I'm
sure the other products they buy are just as badly written. True, they've
cut down on the number of in house COBOL coders but I'm not really sure
whether it's a consequence of moving from the old days where people rarely
bought applications to today where virtually everything is purchased rather
than written in house and even many are supported by the vendor. That's just
a different work model and it doesn't mean COBOL isn't being written. I
agree the landscape has changed quite a bit but I don't think there's one
simple reason and I don't think it's just COBOL.

> I'm not anti-COBOL (quite the contrary; it gave me an excellent living for 
> many years and is still doing so to a much lesser extent. I am a strong 
> advocate of bringing COBOL code into the 21st century (where it is sensible 
> to do so) rather than discarding it. But it needs to be encapsulated so it 
> can integrate properly and so it doesn't need lots of maintenance.)

There are some simple ways to do that, but I agree it's not usually done.

> No, my comment was really historical. Today's processors and data subsytems 
> can usually manage it, but in the early days of the first online systems 
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> with it all.

Yes, that's true.

> Having worked with both CICS and IMS in the early days I agree they are 
> robust transaction processing systems. But even then, there were better TP 
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> how to market it. (cf. VHS and Betamax for videotape...)

I suppose it will sound odd since I have never heard of taskmaster or shadow
but I really can't imagine them (or anything else, really) being more robust
than CICS or IMS today- they're best of breed. Surely that wasn't always the
case but it has been that way since at least the 1980s. People do need to
buy something with solid support, but knowing how critical performance and
RAS are, nobody would buy Blue if something better was around. It all comes
down to money and time is money.

> (but no-one from this forum). At least some are people who found our free 
> COBOL structure analysis tool on the COBOL21 site. (This is the Tool that 
> Robert Wagner

I loved him in "It Takes a Thief!" I was not aware he was so multifaceted!

blame. We could all use a turnaround.
```

```
and low confidence that is really not the fault of the IT department.

tentative and fragile.

>

"Most of all"...???!  I can honestly say I have never approached a system 
implementation with the idea of "What if it doesn't work"?  The question of 
fallbacks and contingencies is addressed LONG before that. Besides, it is my 
job (very often) to make sure that it DOES work. At a programming level, 
would you write a program with the idea in your head: "This will never 
work"? Why would you waste time? If you KNOW it can never work, don't do it. 
If just THINK it MIGHT never work, then fix it so it DOES work. :-)


> And
> those are just the tip of the iceberg. I'm glad I don't make those

I'm glad I don't work in places where that is the process.


>> The trouble with getting programmers to design applications is that
>> they design with a view to what they know technically.
…[12 more quoted lines elided]…
> that's not the way to work but nobody listens.

I described at some length an alternative process. Having tried the 
traditional "standard" (waterfall, SDLC) approaches for years, with somewhat 
mixed results, I was happy to try the new approaches. They work better and 
deliver more timely solutions to the business. For those reasons, I have 
continued using them. It is certainly true that they suit Object Oriented 
Development very well but you COULD bend them to fit procedural COBOL as 
well. The fundamentals are iteration and interaction rather than a serial 
inline process of stages with each stage requiring review and sign off and 
then repeating that if it has to be changed. (Or the famous: "We'll do that 
in the next Release...").
>
>> (How many times in your career have you been told by a programmer:
…[10 more quoted lines elided]…
>

Excellent!
> I snipped it for brevity but I don't see any conflict between
> traditional coding methods and tools with the approach you suggest
…[10 more quoted lines elided]…
> this is the reality in big software companies.

Mine is a very small software company :-) Nevertheless, we have established 
considerable credibility and I have never had a dissatisfied customer or 
anyone who needed dunning to pay their bill to us. BTW, I LOVE developing 
new products and am working on some at the moment... :-)

Sadly, some of it has to go on hold because of the current climate.

> Either that or buy more
> existing products from another company. New product development is
> one of
> the most expensive parts of the business and most "software"
> companies don't want to deal with that part.

And yet it is also the "fun" part and the lifeblood of the company. When I 
first decided to write tools to help me move off COBOL there were many 
people who said what I was proposing could not be done. ("You can't 
automatically modify COBOL code to move from indexed to Relational 
Databases. There are too many possible styles of coding and too many options 
that might have been used. It'll never work."... "You'll never be able to 
normalise a generated database. The best you can do is refelect a COBOL 
record in a table."... "You won't be able to provide a generic object that 
can implement every action a programmer might do against an indexed file"... 
and so on. It didn't happen overnight (I spent a couple of years on it) but 
it DID happen. As other people started using the same tools and I got more 
feedback the tools improved. I firmly believe in the evolution of software 
(iteration and interaction).
>
>> Yep, me too. I like agile style development because I can see if it
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> companies we sell to gets us invited out the door.

Yes, in the mainframe world "agile" has unfortunate connotations for many 
people. They equate it with "No specs."... "Written on the fly"... "not 
properly debugged"... "seat of the pants programming"... harrumph... 
harrumph.

None of the above is entirely true although some of it is partially true 
:-). People who are very reactionary don't even want to find out more.

I encountered this in one company in the U.K. but I persuaded them to try a 
small, non-critical, pilot  (low risk...) on the understanding it would not 
take more than 4 months and if it didn't succeed to everyone's satisfaction 
I would then manage the development using SDLC for as long as it took, at 
half my usual rate. :-)

The business loved it, the programmers loved it and as far as  know this is 
now their standard development approach. (It was around 15 years ago; they 
may have dropped internal development altogether by now.)



> They want stable,
> and it doesn't matter how old it is or how much it costs. It must

:-)

…[5 more quoted lines elided]…

:-) Just saying it is "all theory" doesn't make it so. I posted an example 
of one network I use personally that has achieved 100% uptime  over the past 
several years. How is that "theory" or "unproven"?

As I said before, modern networks have built in redundancy that guarantees 
reliability. You seem to be claiming that ONLY mainframes have reliability. 
My point is that that is simply not the case. The fact that there are now 
reliable networks doesn't mean that mainframes are any less reliable, it 
just means that there are other options which are just as good.


> Cloud or network failures have no
> limits, they affect

remote terminal to ANY computer there is a possibility of the system being 
compromised.


> I haven't seen a hack of a mainframe in 4 decades
> and I don't expect to see
> one.

it is not immune. If you don't want to take my word for it, perhaps IBM can 
persuade you...

http://searchdatacenter.techtarget.com/tip/IBM-mainframe-encryption-The-gold-standard-for-security-does-an-upgrade

Note the conclusion in the above.

Can't get a virus on a mainframe?

https://p8.secure.hostingprod.com/@www.net-q.com/ssl/SecurityIssues/hackingmethod15.pdf

I would agree that, at least for the moment, mainframes are more resistant 
to hacking than a Network. However, efforts to secure private networks are 
not standing still...


>> They don't have to be taken down for "preventive maintenance" they

…[6 more quoted lines elided]…

Actually whether they did or not is irrelevant, because it HAS been proven. 
I saw network recovery at first hand when a digger cut a fibre cable up the 
road from me... :-) I was working on my system and never even knew the cable 
had been cut until some hours later. It recovered within milliseconds. In 
contrast, we had a power failure for a couple of hours caused by a car 
hitting a power pole. I guess electricity must be harder to re-route than 
data streams...

http://www.network-protection.net/multi-layer-network-recovery-restoration-escalation/

…[16 more quoted lines elided]…
> calculations.

I put it in large capitals... look again.

As a C# programmer I have access to over 100,000 Classes of pre-written, 
debugged code. To duplicate this in COBOL would be billions of lines. But 
no-one has attempted to duplicate it in COBOL so it simply isn't available 
unless you use a COBOL .Net compiler.  (The reasons why you wouldn't want to 
do that are more than I will go into here)

I never had to write the .Net libraries (yet I use them every day) so they 
cannot figure in the lines of code I DO have to write. These are the lines 
which must be "maintained".


>And if you don't include locally written classes it's
> clearly not an apples and apples comparison.

I can write a "local" Class in C# in around 1/3 of the lines it would take 
to do it in OO COBOL. But it is a silly argument because the problem is not 
with the lines of code and maintaining them (that is a COBOL view, it isn't 
relevant in OOP...), but rather with the pardigms that C# and COBOL address.

> When it comes to actual
> lines of code doing commercial processing I don't think COBOL can be
> beat,

It HAS been beat. Mine is not the only codebase that shrank considerably 
when we threw COBOL out.

> since it's so ideal for
> reporting,

COBOL is abysmal for reporting when compared to packages like StimulSoft or 
Crystal. Anyone who has ever spent time counting fillers in a COBOL print 
line knows there is no comparison between doing this and simply dragging and 
dropping the field you want, to the place in the line where you want it...

 financial calculations and rounding (despite that other
> thread). All those things take quite a bit of effort in other
> languages.

No they don't. They did once; not any more. There are now decimal classes 
available specifically targeted for commercial processing. Do try and keep 
up... :-)

>Think of all the work picture fields save you, and the
> automatic conversion between
> packed and binary and display fields etc. No other language does that
> as easily, certainly not Java or C#.

I vehemently disagree. There are new facilites in C# v4 which you may not be 
familiar with, but evern without those, it is not a problem to convert data 
types in C#. (I haven't written Java for a about 6 months so I don't know 
what's available there.)


>You don't have to cast fields or
> call conversion routines in COBOL, you just MOVE one field to
> another, and the compiler does the work once, instead of every time
> you process a transaction in Java/C#.

I agree that COBOL is good in this regard, but so is C#. Casting is simple 
and just part of the syntax. I don't understand your objection to it.

Conversion to display doesn't even require an explicit cast

  displayString = compField.ToString();

>

> Surely that wasn't always the case but it has been that way since at
> least the 1980s. People do need to
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> comes down to money and time is money.

"Nobody would buy Blue if something better was around..."   Sorry, I have to 
smile.

I grew up in an era when IBM was the only game in town. It would have been 
impossible to further my career UNLESS I had IBM experience on my CV, so I 
made a point of getting it. But I also made  a point of working with other 
equipment. Burroughs, Univac, NCR, Control Data Corporation, Honeywell, and 
ICL, all before PCs were readily available in the workplace.

I can report unequivocally that several of the companies listed had 
"something better" than IBM, yet some of them are no longer trading and 
others have merged for survival.

I have seen very competent IT managers almost lose their jobs and have their 
competence questioned because they bought something other than IBM. The 
argument ran: "If your IT Director can't see that IBM is the best, he must 
be incompetent."

Hopefully, you have never heard the expression: "Nobody ever got fired for 
buying IBM".  It's true. Even if the whole thing went pear-shaped, the buyer 
could go to the Board and say: "Hey, I bought IBM. What more could I do?" In 
the days before PC networks computers were more often selected on the basis 
of politics than on the basis of what was best or even "better"...

The fact that a certain product has pre-eminence in the market place does 
NOT mean that there isn't "something better". It means the product is 
acceptable and it is well marketed and supported.

I should hasten to add that the IBM of today, is NOT the company described 
above. They successfully re-invented themselves under much better management 
when the whole house of cards came tumbling down after the arrival of PCs 
(which, ironically, they invented...)

CICS and IMS are both good products (although I always liked IMS/DC better 
than CICS) but they are not as flexible, responsive to dynamic load 
variances, or as scalable as a distributed network.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: 3270 emulator?

_(reply depth: 14)_

- **From:** Michael Wojcik <mwojcik@newsguy.com>
- **Date:** 2011-06-24T11:48:07-04:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<iu2c300240d@news3.newsguy.com>`
- **In-Reply-To:** `<2bd64c02c13607976d90236dc4884f12@dizum.com>`
- **References:** `<962kljFqisU1@mid.individual.net> <2bd64c02c13607976d90236dc4884f12@dizum.com>`

```
Nomen Nescio wrote:
> "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:
>> There seems to be a popular misconception that only mainframes can process 
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
> quite the opposite.

While these sorts of sweeping generalizations are not particularly
meaningful or useful - key terms like "transactions", "securely",
"performance", etc have not been defined, and there's no context
supplied - it's true that there are formally-proven theoretical
differences between tightly-coupled and loosely-coupled architectures,
for transaction-processing purposes.

For example there's the CAP Theorem, which shows that in a network
it's impossible to guarantee all three of consistency, atomicity, and
tolerance to partitioning (a break in the network).

CAP isn't a problem for tightly-coupled systems because they offer
greater resistance to partitioning, and they don't claim partitioning
tolerance as a feature (which proponents of loosely-coupled systems
generally do).

Similarly, there are advantages to loosely-coupled systems that cannot
be realized by tightly-coupled ones.

Without understanding the large bodies of formal and empirical work
that have been developed in comparing the characteristics of
tightly-coupled and loosely-coupled systems, there's not much point in
making absolute claims about what can or cannot be realized in either
environment.

> I
> haven't seen a hack of a mainframe in 4 decades and I don't expect to see
> one.

That doesn't mean it hasn't happened: CHRISTMA EXEC, for example. More
importantly, security issues with in-house backend systems that have
little public visibility don't tend to be advertised. As always,
absence of evidence is not evidence of absence.

> That design hasn't been proven,

This is the sort of claim I referred to above. It's really not a
useful contribution to the debate. "Proven" in what sense? Have you
reviewed the literature regarding packet-switched multipath network
tolerance to failure?

> nor have any of the failovers worked without
> hitches and glitches.

How would you know? In fact, the Internet successfully accommodates
numerous disruptive events on a daily basis.

>> If we examine your very interesting last sentence regarding lines of code 
>> and complexity needing to be minimised, COBOL loses again. Object systems 
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> libraries, I think you need to factor those in to your calculations.

Or use them in your COBOL program.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: 3270 emulator?

- **References:** `<962kljFqisU1@mid.individual.net> <2bd64c02c13607976d90236dc4884f12@dizum.com> <iu2c300240d@news3.newsguy.com> <96kenbFv64U1@mid.individual.net>`

```
Pete Dashwood wrote:
> Michael Wojcik wrote:
>>
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
> (Except for claims based on actual empirical experience and documented...)

Such claims are of limited use to readers without access to that
evidence and documentation - and the documentation ought to include
methodology and other critical information. Of course anecdotes can
serve as examples (or counterexamples), and they're useful in that
sense, but I think we need to be careful in extrapolating from them.

And even in a rigorous empirical study, *absolute* claims are
problematic, since generalizations drawn from individual cases are
likely to be suspect.

It's clearly true that large business systems can be run on large
monolithic systems of various types; on large monolithic systems
partitioned into multiple virtual systems; on large distributed
systems treated as distributed; on large distributed systems treated
as a single system image; on large distributed systems partitioned
into virtual machines; and so on. We've seen large business systems
run on all of those architectures.

Really interesting and successful business systems have been built
from networked, heterogeneous architectures since at least the early
1980s. I was working for a middleware company back in the mid-80s,
building software for serious distributed business applications.

What the formal and rigorous empirical research has shown is that the
differences among those architectures are complicated and sometimes
counterintuitive - certainly counter to some of the excessive claims
that sometimes get advanced by marketing folks, trade-press pundits,
etc (no surprise there).

My concern is that discussions of monolithic versus distributed
systems tend to produce more heat than light, particularly when people
aren't aware of, or ignore, the ample research in the area.
```

```
Pete Dashwood wrote:
> 
> During the past 3 years my usage of Virtual Machine technology has 
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> image.

Yes. Cheap (free for purchase and low in effort) virtual machines are
one of the few real, significant improvements in the PC programming
experience in the last decade.

> It wouldn't be too hard to implement a Linux image.

Not hard at all, with one of the recent new-user-friendly
distributions (eg Ubuntu). Typically it's just a question of download
the CD-ROM image, create a new VM, mount the image as a virtual CD for
the machine, and start it up. It'll install itself and let you get
going; then as you learn more about it, you can install optional
components and such.

You should be up and running in less than an hour.

Of course, the GUI UI, tools, and applications that you see by default
in those distributions only give you a small part of the Linux
experience. It's entirely possible to use Linux (like all the
Unix-derived OSes) without ever touching a GUI. I don't use GUIs for
any of my professional Linux or Unix development - that's all
command-line. When I fire up my play Linux VM, I have the GUI (X11
with KDE, just because I haven't bothered switching window managers
yet), but I mostly use it to arrange command-line shell windows.

The main GUI app I run on Linux is Firefox. Once in a while I use
something else that justifies a GUI, like Gimp (image processing) or
LyX (typeset documents).

But while I think anyone who wants to understand Linux should get some
experience with a shell (probably the bash shell, which is the default
in most Linux distributions), it is possible to do pretty much
anything in the GUI, should that be your preference.
```

```
Michael Wojcik wrote:
> Pete Dashwood wrote:

Thanks Michael.

I am coming out of the lethargy I have been experiencing over the past few 
weeks and getting interested in doing work again :-)

My problem is that, obviously, paid work has to take priority, and PRIMA 
also has a high priority demand on my time.

I have been planning to look at Linux for some time now but unless I can 
make money out of it, it is a luxury I can't quite afford. :-) I can invest 
time in learning phone technology because there is a payoff. Obviously, at 
some point, I can afford to indulge myself without direct reward and that 
will be when I get to Linux.

Although I started computing in an age when there was no GUI and I never had 
a problem with command lines, nowadays, I confess that I prefer the GUI. And 
the new generation of GUIs which are appearing I find quite exciting. Many 
years ago, when applications were "locked in" to a particular platform (and 
COBOL was a blazing beacon for "transportability") I remember getting the 
idea to separate things like data access from business logic because it was 
costing us an arm and a leg every time IBM did a new OS release, 
(modifications to apps, regression testing, and so on). I came up with a 
system (initially for British Aerospace) which would allow their diverse 
platforms to run the same (COBOL developed) applications, without change, 
because the user interface and the data access were implemented through an 
interface that remained consistent. (later this became the multi-tiered 
approach that is a standard part of Network system development to this day. 
I'm not claiming responsibility for the 3 tier model, but I did have exactly 
the same idea while relaxing in a hot mineral pool on holiday back in NZ 
:-)) I called this system a "process for interfacing to machines" (PRIMA). 
It evolved for me into the recognition of layers and objects as being 
fundamental building blocks for successful Network based systems.

Pete.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: 3270 emulator?

_(reply depth: 10)_

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2011-06-18T01:36:41+12:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<9613jaFod0U1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<94qhabFoamU1@mid.individual.net> <957notFl6hU1@mid.individual.net> <79519b48-e641-424b-b821-20369f12a42b@v10g2000yqn.googlegroups.com> <isonid48nm@news4.newsguy.com> <95apcjFlgnU1@mid.individual.net> <isre5i12k44@news3.newsguy.com> <95d09qF9g9U1@mid.individual.net> <it561e1jpo@news1.newsguy.com> <95o0deF9mgU1@mid.individual.net> <itdo3q12lcd@news2.newsguy.com>`

```
Michael Wojcik wrote:
> Pete Dashwood wrote:
>>
…[30 more quoted lines elided]…
> it has any necessary relationship with GUIs, though.

Not with GUIs per se...

But that is the whole point. If you have a "presentation layer" it is 
interfaced to from a buffer in memory. Your application shares that buffer 
with the presentation software. If it is CICS, the buffer may be defined 
using BMS, if it is Windows it may use windows Forms or WPF.

If you are using OO you may write a Class to do some getting and setting of 
data to/from text box controls, but the data is placed in the buffer. 
Programs do NOT reference GUI controls, instead, they invoke methods of a 
presentation layer Class. In exactly the same way that separation is 
maintained from the database (none of my COBOL stuff contains embedded SQL; 
it all invokes Object methods of a DAL (Data Access Layer). The objects 
comprising this layer are generated by the PRIMA Migration Toolset, so I 
don't even have to write them, just use them...), so is separation 
maintained by use of a presentation layer for the user interface.

The DAL used by PRIMA has been published and is documented here:

http://primacomputing.co.nz/cobol21/ShowMe.aspx?ptitle=DAL Programmer's 
Guide&pcontent=documents/DAL Manual.pdf
(As you can see, it carries out every action that is possible against an 
indexed dataset, but does it on a Relational database. Each DAL object is 
generated to support a given table set so it knows all the key and data 
structures for that table set, all the repeating groups, redefines, group 
levels and data types used by the COBOL record definitions, and can easily 
populate the COBOL record layout from the database, and vice versa. That is 
all fully automatic. But it is a simple matter to tailor other objects that 
operate on different tablesets and views in a specific manner, as well. You 
might do this to improve performance or for any number of other reasons, but 
none of it involves changes to business logic.)

If I wanted to move an application from Windows or .Net Forms  to WPF I 
could do so very easily without having to rewrite the application. 
Similarly, if I liked a particular third party GUI system or even wanted to 
write my own GUI in Java Chrome, no problem. The interface is unchanged. All 
it needs is the data used by the applications, the rest is smoke and mirrors 
and properly has no place within the Business logic. All that stuff can be 
written into the presentation layer.

I'm starting to think about a presentation layer using HTML5 and 
Javascript... (guess why :-)?) and it will have no impact on existing code. 
In the same way that I have procedural COBOL using .Net Forms as a 
presentation layer (via a C# wrapper) it won't be too hard to build a HTML5 
Javascript GUI. that can talk to the same business and DAL layers.

(Many years ago, about the time that PRIMA was set up I wrote a "screen 
painter" tool that allowed you to define fixed and variable screens  by 
"painting" them how you wanted (in character mode - it was for COBOL). You 
hit a button and generated a CICS BMS buffer, an IMS/DC buffer and  a PC 
screen section so that exactly the same interface could be presented on PCs 
and mainframes. I almost sold it to Fujitsu, Australia, for a very large sum 
of money, but the deal fell through at the last minute due to circumstances 
beyond their and my control. The point is that separation (tiered 
architecture) has been a Good Thing for a very long time now and it doesn't 
matter whether you do it with GUI or with characters.

There was some resistance from the "shop floor" when I required that there 
be no embedded SQL in PRIMA applications which utilised OO COBOL components. 
Since then myself and my occasional helpers have been playing with LINQ and 
realised it is just streets ahead of SQL (even though it currently generates 
SQL beneath the covers.) As it became clear that we could implement a LINQ 
based solution if we choose to, with very little effort, there has been much 
less grumbling in the ranks... :-) At the moment that is still a luxury (I 
would LOVE to have the DAL generated to support LINQ instead of SQL, but 
there is SOME work involved and, as everything runs fine in SQL there is no 
pressing reason to change it.)

Objects and layers.

Pete.
```

```
Pete Dashwood wrote:
> Michael Wojcik wrote:
>> I agree that separation of concerns is a good idea. I don't see that
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
> using BMS, if it is Windows it may use windows Forms or WPF.

Well, that rather depends on the application design and implementation.

For example, there's no shared in-memory buffer for a CICS
application; there are buffers (TIOAs) owned by BMS on behalf of the
application, and there's a buffer in the terminal or
terminal-emulation software. Data is exchanged between those over a
communications link of some sort (SNA, TCP/IP, etc), with more buffers
involved.

If your presentation layer is HTML, then the user agent (web browser)
has a buffer, and your application has a buffer (probably), and the
web server (if it's not integrated into your application) has a
buffer; and there are network buffers and so forth.

> If you are using OO you may write a Class to do some getting and setting of 
> data to/from text box controls, but the data is placed in the buffer. 
> Programs do NOT reference GUI controls, instead, they invoke methods of a 
> presentation layer Class.

One would certainly hope that an OO application would abstract
presentation-layer details. Of course it's possible to write poor code
in any environment, and there are plenty of C++ programs that write
willy-nilly to cout and so forth.

But again this isn't specific to GUIs. I've just finished implementing
a bunch of BMS functionality for our .NET implementation of CICS. All
OO, with proper abstractions and separation of concerns.

Similarly, our administrative tools for this product come in GUI and
command-line forms (in fact two of the latter, since there are both
regular commands and Powershell cmdlets), but they all use an
appropriate presentation layer to invoke presentation-independent
management objects.

This is all normal practice, in my book.

And it's possible to achieve abstraction and separation of concerns
without OO - you just typically end up reinventing OO in the process.
(I have a lot of C code that effectively implements its own OO
mechanisms.) OO languages just make it easier by providing a richer
set of abstraction features.

It need not be classes, either. ECMAScript (Javascript et al) is an OO
language but not a class-based one, for example. And in functional
languages you can achieve the features of OO without using an OO model.
```

```
Pete Dashwood wrote:
> Michael Wojcik wrote:
>> And it's possible to achieve abstraction and separation of concerns
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
> with this.

Sure. OO was invented more or less as a way to achieve abstraction and
separation of concerns in a consistent, logical manner, and to save
programmers from having to reinvent those mechanisms all the time.
There are other ways to do it, but it boils down to four choices:

- Use an OO language and OO approach to achieve abstraction
- Reinvent OO mechanisms in a non-OO language
- Use one of the less-common approaches for abstraction
- Don't use abstraction at all (and end up with a mess of
highly-coupled, fragile code)

Since the OO approach is very common and well-known, that's the one
most people think of and go to. And that's fine - using an approach
that's well-understood and well-documented, and will be understood by
maintainers, is a good thing.

>> It need not be classes, either. ECMAScript (Javascript et al) is an OO
>> language but not a class-based one, for example. And in functional
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
> that Java is and C# (where everything is a class).

Up until the current ECMAScript draft, it doesn't have classes at all,
in fact. (And the class support in the current ECMAScript draft is of
dubious utility.) ECMAScript is a "prototype-based" OO language - a
model it inherited from an obscure predecessor named Self. Every
ECMAScript object has a "prototype" object it inherits from. You can
change the prototype object, and anything its descendants inherit will
also be changed. When you try to access a property (member or method)
of an object, if it's not present in the object itself, the language
walks up the prototype chain, trying to find it.

Some people feel this model is inadequate for large projects. It's
never been a problem for me, but I understand the issues. There's a
fair bit of invisible coupling, for example - you have to be careful
when you change a method in one object that you're not breaking other
objects that might inherit from it.

> For web pages the
> "Classes" I use are normally COM components and they get instantiated and 
> activated in a couple of lines of Javascript.

Those actually are class-based objects. Of course, then you're no
longer working in ECMAScript, and you lose portability, among other
things.
```

```
Charles Hottel wrote:
> 
> What are the other "less-common approaches for abstraction"?  Thanks. 

Well, OO abstracts by collecting associcated data ("members") and
operations on it ("methods") into abstract data types ("objects",
which in a typed OO language are typically defined by types called
"classes"). It does that to achieve three main goals:

- encapsulation (code that uses an object only has to know about its
public interface, not its internal implementation)

- inheritance (an object can be enhanced or specialized without losing
its generic features)

- polymorphism (objects with the same generic features can be treated
as generic objects, ignoring their specialized features)

You can achieve some or all of those three things without taking the
OO route of packaging data and operations up into objects. (Though
some people would call any abstraction mechanism that achieves these
"OO", so to some extent it's a matter of definition.)

Encapsulation is easy, even in procedural languages with no OO
features. Just provide an API and some sort of opaque data type (a
"descriptor" or "handle" or whatever you want to call it); the caller
does everything through the API, and implementation details are
hidden. Many OSes do this for their system interfaces.

Another approach is to require that all your data types decompose into
a collection of primitive types you know how to handle, along with
structural constructs you can handle in a generic fashion. This is
typically done with self-describing data, or tagged data combined with
a type dictionary of some sort. Now the operations are separate from
the data (so it's not OO), but you can still have some support for
inheritance and polymorphism, with some loss of encapsulation. It also
solves the multiple-dispatch problem, because methods are no longer
"owned" by an object, so when multiple objects interact there's no
issue of figuring out which of them "owns" the action. But this is
pretty primitive, and it often leads to excessive coupling.

Allotyping is another possibility. Allotyping is often used in OO
languages, but it can be used separately from OO (depending on how
strictly you define OO). With an allotyped language, objects send
messages to one another; if an object knows how to handle a message,
it does so. This gets rid of the inheritance hierarchy and the issues
of multiple inheritance / interfaces / mixins.

You can impose a lot of abstraction with the combination of
first-class functions, polytypes, and pattern matching, as in the ML
language family (Standard ML, Caml and OCaml, Haskell, etc). Features
like these let you implement generics at runtime, rather than
compilation time.

Declarative languages, like SQL, let you express goals without knowing
(many of the) implementation details; the engine figures out how to
get from what it has to what you want. That's a form of abstraction,
too, and it actually incorporates some elements of inheritance and
polymorphism, though usually in a specialized way. This approach is
more typical of domain-specific languages.

Basically the point is that the strongly-typed members-and-methods OO
style isn't the only way to get abstraction.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: 3270 emulator?

_(reply depth: 17)_

- **From:** Michael Wojcik <mwojcik@newsguy.com>
- **Date:** 2011-06-28T08:37:39-04:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<iucibl12qgi@news1.newsguy.com>`
- **In-Reply-To:** `<LOKdnZBvOeaAPpvTnZ2dnUVZ_j2dnZ2d@earthlink.com>`

```
Charles Hottel wrote:
> 
> How did you come to use the term "allotyping"?  It seems to beused mostly as 
> a medical term.

It may well be used more often in medicine, but I've seen it in
computer science, specifically in the theory of programming languages.
That's where I got it from.

Informally, in a programming context, some people use "duck typing" to
mean the same thing. That's derived from the old "if it looks like a
duck and sounds like a duck..." saying.

>  I am guessing that your use of it means that the object
> performs  a test for compatibility on the messages that it receives.  Is 
> that correct? 

That's pretty much the idea, though it can also be the message-passing
infrastructure that makes the decision.

Basically, with conventional hierarchical OO languages like C++ and
Java, messages are implemented as subroutine calls, and an object can
"accept a message" (be the target of a subroutine call) if it
implements the method being called, or inherits an implementation from
a class higher in the class hierarchy.

Java interfaces are basically a language mechanism to let a class say
"hey, I also accept these messages". (C++ doesn't have interfaces
because it supports multiple inheritance, but the trend these days
among OO languages is for single inheritance with interfaces or
mix-ins or a similar feature.)

Allotyped languages don't use the class hierarchy to decide what
objects can accept what messages. When one object tries to send a
message to another, either the receiving object or the language
infrastructure says: does the recipient handle a message that looks
like this? If so, the message is sent and processed.

ECMAScript ("Javascript") is an allotyped language. ECMAScript objects
are just associative arrays - a set of name/value pairs. Methods are
names that are associated with executable code. When you try to invoke
a method on an object in ECMAScript, it looks to see if the object has
a value under that name, and if the value is a function.

Allotyped languages typically trade some compile-time type safety for
flexibility.
```

```
Michael Wojcik wrote:
> Pete Dashwood wrote:
>> Michael Wojcik wrote:
…[24 more quoted lines elided]…
>

I have some occasional part-time help with PRIMA code (which is all in C#) 
and there has never been a problem with communicating what the code does or 
what it needs to do.  Despite not being a "self-documenting" language like 
COBOL, C# is succinct and clear. (We also comment anything that's a bit 
different from the usual.). The C# guys I use have no problem with the code 
but are amused with COBOL and pass that to me on the very rare occasions 
that it makes sense to amend or extend a COBOL component. Lately I have 
taken to rewriting these components in C# unless they are really complex and 
implement existing functionality that I really don't want to mess with... 
:-))

>>> It need not be classes, either. ECMAScript (Javascript et al) is an
>>> OO language but not a class-based one, for example. And in
…[23 more quoted lines elided]…
> objects that might inherit from it.

I guess that has always been a problem with inheritance. It might be safer 
to code an override if there is any uncertainty, but that kind of defeats 
the purpose of reusability.

>
>> For web pages the
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> Those actually are class-based objects.

Yes, I realise that :-)



>Of course, then you're no
> longer working in ECMAScript, and you lose portability, among other
> things.

I disagree. (Not with the essence of your statement which is strictly true, 
but it is of more practical use to me to be able to invoke Classes FROM 
Javascript (even if that means I no longer have a pure Javascript solution). 
There has been much talk about replacing COM but so far it is still alive 
and well. I have NEVER had a case where I couldn't transport a COM component 
into any environment I wanted to, so portability for me is not an issue. I 
have programs and scripts written in C#, Java, Javascript, VBScript, and 
COBOL, ALL able to invoke the SAME COM component, and run it from a desktop, 
across a network (COM++), or from a web page. I've been writing COM 
components for around 15 years now and I really like this technology. Having 
said that, I recently had to come to grips with it in a Micro Focus COBOL 
environment and it was really ugly. Fujitsu provides a COM class which puts 
most of this ugliness (and tedium) under the covers. I found the Net Express 
5.1 documentation contradictory,  (especially with regard to the use of 
trigger files) and there was no batch compile facility for COM. (I had to 
develop my own which involved creating .MAK, .IDL and .REG files and using 
the NMAKE facility to batch compile them. I was very impressed with the 
support I received from the Micro Focus community forum and so I posted the 
final solution there for anyone else who needs to batch compile COM 
components in a Micro Focus NE 5.1 environment. I also posted some 
observations based on my experience with Micro Focus NE 5.1. Here's the 
link:

http://community.microfocus.com/Forums/3_COBOL__Net_Express_Server_Express_Visual_COBOL/413_Is_anyone_here_actually_using_IN_Process_COM_Server_DLLs?Page=2

I am much more concerned with practicality than I am with theory (although I 
am also very interested in theory as a means to achieving practicality :-) 
That's one reason I always find your posts interesting, Michael.)

Currently, I don't care if a solution is in say, Javascript, and also 
requires one or more COM components to run properly.  I find this an ideal 
way to encapsulate functionality for reuse and my solutions lever it to the 
maximum. I see no problem with using scripting (javascript or VBscript or 
even WSH) as "glue" to hold discrete components together and ensure control 
of them.

I take your point that doing this means the solution is no longer purely 
ECMAScript. It may be that as I get more into phone and win 8 I will want 
purer solutions, but up to now building things as components and ensuring 
they can run anywhere has worked very well for me.

Pete.
```

```
It may not be an issue for you, but that doesn't mean you haven't lost

platforms. COM runs on Windows, period.

Leaving portability off your list of requirements is a valid choice.
But you can't claim that invoking COM from ECMAScript doesn't discard
ECMAScript's portability - it's manifestly true that it does.
```

```
Alistair Maclean wrote:
> On Jun 7, 11:41 pm, "Pete Dashwood"
> <dashw...@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:
…[9 more quoted lines elided]…
> so they might be a little biased.

I don't think mainframes will be dead in five years. They may never die at 
all, but simply be relegated to "high end" service of the Network.

You are completely right about people with a vested interest making 
predictions. This is one reason I was sceptical of the famous Gartner 
reports about lines of COBOL. (The other reason was that the numbers simply 
didn't add up...) Gartner's figures were well received by the people they 
wrote them for because it was what those people wanted to hear. Later, as 
jobs started disappearing, questions were raised and Gartner withdrew their 
report. (Notice that they still provide reports to industry and don't seem 
to have suffered any loss of credibility over this...I guess they deserve 
credit because at least they accepted they were wrong and corrected it.)

I do it because it is fun; I get no financial reward whether I'm right or 
wrong. (I suppose, if I get it right, I have some advantage in being aware 
of what is happening and getting onto the gravy train while seats are still 
available :-)) but beyond that, I don't consciously try to predict as a 
means to get rich. (If I did, I have failed dismally...:-))  For me, trying 
to detect emerging trends and technologies which are likely to affect the 
general IT industry is both fun and interesting. It also forces me to view 
the "wider picture" than the one simple area of technology I'm working with.

This morning I viewed a video about the effects of technology on the Casey 
Anthony trial. Mobile devices, social networks, the instant "communication" 
which is becoming woven into the fabric of our society may mean it is 
impossible for anyone to get a fair trial any more and it is almost 
impossible to protect a jury from seeing what public opinion is. Instead of 
being tried by 12 people who are privy to the evidence and legal argument, 
it may be that we get tried by 12 million people who all have their own spin 
and agenda and who may be severely uninformed on the actual facts, rather 
than the hearsay.

(I would stress that I have no opinion about whether she is innocent or 
guilty, but I do have some concerns about how Justice is going to cope with 
an inextricably connected and intertwined population who update each other 
every minute...)

The bottom line is that the world has been changed by the technology that 
many here spent their lives working with. The "Age of COBOL" was a 
foundation which has evolved into the "Age of the Network". The Internet has 
woven itself into our lives, almost while we weren't looking, and the 
implications are still emerging.

As long as I can remember I have always hoped that the power of computers 
would be made available to people without the need for a technocracy or 
"priesthood" working as intermediaries between the need for applications and 
the actual provision of them. While we are not there yet, visible progress 
has been made and, as my own career draws to a close, I'm pretty happy with 
the state and direction of IT today.

I never thought the impetus would come from mobile communications technology 
though.

Pete.
```

```
Howard Brazee wrote:
> On Thu, 9 Jun 2011 14:10:18 +1200, "Pete Dashwood"
> <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:
…[14 more quoted lines elided]…
> being right.

I'd like to disagree about that but I can't. :-)

However, I have seen and worked with some managers who were courageous 
enough to take responsibility for their decisions (and encouraged their 
staff to do the same). It tends to create a much better "culture" in the 
organization and this results in increased productivity, which is a 
fundamental goal of most businesses. The spin off is that such places are 
much more pleasant to work in, absenteeism is low and morale is generally 
better. People feel that doing their job actually makes a difference and 
there are often incentives for individuals and teams.

I have to say that if you talk about "Government" (local and national) there 
is not much of this to be seen. I wondered about it a few years back. Why 
should it be so? People who work in Giovernment are not less capable, 
neither are they more "timid" than people who work in industry.

The conclusion I reached was the fact that Government punishes failure more 
than industry does. The secure job, pension plan, almost automatic promotion 
through grades, means that employees simply don't want to take the risk of 
having their card marked and thereby losing a grade or promotion. If you can 
stall a decision until it is overtaken by events you cannot be seen to have 
decided incorrectly.

I do recall once being told by the CEO of a company where I had been 
contracted to do a fairly critical job: "Now, Peter, I know everybody makes 
mistakes, but if you fuck this up...." I didn't. And it is the only time I 
remember being threatened. :-)

Pete.
```

```
> fingers to zoom in on a web page or image, and swiping left or right to turn
> a page or switch between full screen apps.

Gestures have been around for many years. Prior to the wide use of
touch screens the gestures were made with the mouse. For example in
Opera. This included zooming and 'page turning'.


> (Points 6 and 7 were demonstrated in the Win 8 video link previously posted.
> The point is that this is NOT a particular vendor's technology; it is across
> the board.)

Yes WP7 and W8 copies much of what others have done.

> 8. All Mac notebooks ship with Multi-Touch trackpads and desktop Macs can
> use Apple's Magic Trackpad.
>
> 9. Mac OS X Lion features full screen apps to take advantage of the entire
> display

Interestingly so does Unity and Gnome 3 which are already shipped.

Actually I tried Gnome 3 and didn't like it for various reasons but
fortunately Linux has many Window Managers so I installed XFCE which
works for me.

> 11. You can swipe to get your desktop to zoom out to display your open
> windows grouped by app, thumbnails of your full screen apps and your
> Dashboard.
>
> (These "thumbnails" correspond to "tiles" in the Win 8 video).

The point about W8 'tiles' (remember Windows 1 was tiled) is that they
are intended to be active, showing for example the latest tweet or
facebook 'be my friend'.


> 13. The Launchpad feature makes it easier to find and launch apps, and with
> a single multi-touch gesture, all your apps are displayed in a full screen
> layout.

I hate that. Hundreds of little pictures.  I never liked the Win95
desktop model, I never see my desktop, and don't have any use for it.
It is always covered by the several apps that I usually have running.
Win98 'active desktop' was worse.

Granted that the launcher (and Gnome 3 'activities' and others)
arrives 'over the top' as it were, but this is simmilar to Winkey+D to
drop and raise all app windows.


> 14. You can organize apps into any order or into folders and swipe through
> unlimited pages of apps.

I never want 'pages of apps'. The icons are a nuisance to me. If I
have to read the titles to know which is the app I want then I just
want the titles and nothing else.

> 15. There's a redesigned Mail app with widescreen layout.

Another annoyance is that monitors are now almost all 1920x1080 for HD
video playback. Application windows tend to have title bars, menu,
toolbars at the top and status bars at the bottom reducing the height
available for the work area. KWord, for example, can be configured
with these to one side or the other leaving maximum height for the
document which is nice. Many others cannot be changed.


> It may be that the mouse is becoming redundant.

I can't reach my screen from where I sit so I will still use a mouse
from time to time, or possibly a trackpad.


> Usually it takes a few years for what people are using at home to appear in
> the Enterprise. As familiarity with the new interfaces is gained, pressure
> to have them in the workplace mounts.

You may notice that Apple do not target the enterprise and are
primarily interested in the consumer market. WP7 is also for consumers
(as said by Microsoft).

However, iPads and iPhones are already established in the 'workplace'.
Some may even run the 3270 emulator that is available for the iPad.


> Many enterprise applications are being
> outsourced or replaced by packages and the vendors want their software to
> have maximum appeal. They therefore implement what are generally accepted
> interfaces.

Certainly there are two things that a software package must do: 1) get
the decision maker to buy it and 2) do useful work.

These may be incompatible requirements.

Different applications require different types of user interface. Just
because the OS has a particular type of UI does not mean that it is
suitable for every type of application.


> The days when enterprise software was developed in-house by a COBOL shop,
> and that shop dictated to the business how the interface would work, are
…[8 more quoted lines elided]…
> computer, and that includes computers in the workplace.

User interfaces on business applications will continue to be set by
the business owners and managers who pay the bills.


> I'll stick my neck out here and say that by 2015 (my predicted date for the
> demise of COBOL as a major development language, although this has already
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
> on home computers and cell phones.

That 'interface technology' is already in the enterprise. There are
types of businesses that would use it. For example where images were
worked on, perhaps for publishing and some others.

COBOL, however, tends not to be used for those sort of apps. It tends
to continue to be used for the applications which are primarily data
entry and processing. Gestures don't get used to enter quantities
required by customers, it is not useful to have 'voice response' when
you also have to talk on the phone to the customer.

Switching to another task can be done with Alt-tab when your hands are
already on the keyboard. Switch to a mouse, trackpad or touch screen
takes too long.

On the other hand I wrote a fully graphical container ship loading
application in COBOL* 20 years ago. Graph output of stability, bending
and shearing, diagramatic representation of fuel tanks, even a
perspective view from the helm. Drag and drop containers from the
terminal stacks to the container bays, etc.

I was writing HTML and JavaScript frontends to COBOL backends (CGI) in
the mid 90s.

You conflate COBOL with 'green screen' and contrast this with latest
wizzy shiny stuff not yet available when there is a whole range that
is between these.


* I started when the early release of MF for Windows 3.0 was first
available, previously I had written simple testing programs in C for
Windows 2.0.
```
